Defendants also selectively omitted the seller's name and address from certain business records. Defendants were given misdemeanor sentences reduced to probation. On appeal, the court held that Cal. Penal Code § 496a was not a strict liability offense and that the jury had not been instructed that it was. Cal. Penal Code § 496a was not void for vagueness because the statutory phrase "ordinarily used by" an entity "engaged in furnishing public utility service" gave a person of reasonable intelligence notice of the prohibited conduct. The state's use of expert testimony to establish defendants' lack of due diligence was not prejudicial error. The court affirmed defendants' convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant junk metal dealers' misdemeanor convictions for violating a statute that forbade junk dealers from buying public utility meters as scrap without first using due diligence to ascertain the seller's authority to dispose of the meters because the state statute criminalizing defendants' behavior was not unconstitutionally vague.

 

1871 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kellam v. Eu


Civ. No. 52688


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


83 Cal. App. 3d 463; 147 Cal. Rptr. 884; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1779



July 31, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 197866, Charles H. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant secretary of state sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), that issued a peremptory writ of mandate which placed respondent independent candidate on a special election ballot without gathering 500 signatures as required by Cal. Elec. Code § 6831. Appellant contended that respondent had not exhibited the broad public support needed to be on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: Appellant secretary of state challenged the trial court's order that issued a peremptory writ of mandate to place respondent independent candidate on the ballot in a special election without his first collecting 500 signatures as required by Cal. Elec. Code § 6831. The court reversed and found that although the special election had passed, the issues were capable of repetition and were not moot. The court noted that Cal. Elec. Code § 7201 established qualifications for independent candidates in special elections and specifically referenced other statutory provisions. The court determined that § 6831 was applicable under the statutory scheme and coincided with appellant's interpretation of the statute that required respondent to obtain 500 signatures, while established party candidates needed only to collect 40. The court found that the state had a right to require the demonstration of broad public support, and that established parties established support by their performance in the previous election and it was not unduly restrictive to require newly formed political parties to show public support by the collection of 500 signatures or one percent of the electorate.


OUTCOME: An order that granted a peremptory writ of mandate to require appellant secretary of state to place respondent independent candidate on a special election ballot was reversed because the statutory scheme required candidates to establish broad public support by collecting 500 signatures, and because it was not an undue burden to require new parties to show broad support by the collection of 500 signatures.

 

1872 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Libertarian Party v. Eu


Civ. No. 52886


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


83 Cal. App. 3d 470; 147 Cal. Rptr. 888; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1780



July 31, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. 202085, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: However, because the statute is unconstitutional, we are required to reverse the judgment of dismissal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, independent candidate, voters, and political party, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained defendant state officials' general demurrer and dismissed plaintiffs' complaint, which alleged that Cal. Elec. Code § 7203 unconstitutionally infringed voters' rights to vote. Plaintiffs contended that an unfair burden was placed on independent candidates.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff independent candidate was the only independent candidate in a field of 13, and placed 11th, in a special primary election called to fill an assembly vacancy. No candidate received a majority of the votes cast. Accordingly, pursuant to Cal. Elec. Code § 7203, the leading candidate from each of the Democrat and Republican parties was named on the general election ballot. Plaintiff independent candidate's name was omitted from the general election ballot because he was not a member of a "qualified political party." Plaintiffs, independent candidate, voters, and political party, filed a petition for writ of mandate to defendant state officials to invalidate § 7203 or, in the alternative, to order plaintiff independent candidate's name included on the ballot. The court reversed a dismissal of the action. Section 7203 unfairly imposed a burden on independent candidates that was not imposed on party-affiliated candidates, and unconstitutionally infringed the right to vote, whether tested under the "strict scrutiny" or "rational basis" test.


OUTCOME: The judgment dismissing the complaint of plaintiffs, independent candidate, voters, and political party, was reversed, in their complaint alleging unconstitutionality of a statute against defendant state officials. The statute that limited the candidates who could be named on the general election ballot in the event no candidate received a majority of votes in a primary election was unconstitutional.

 

1873 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


San Francisco Labor Council v. University of California


Civ. No. 41756.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


83 Cal. App. 3d 198; 147 Cal. Rptr. 790; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1754



July 27, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Aug. 24, 1978 (see 86 C.A.3d 863)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 712768, John E. Benson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant labor councils sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), that sustained the demurrer without leave to amend of respondent college regents, and denied the request of appellants for a mandamus to compel respondents to comply with the prevailing wage law contained in Cal. Educ. Code § 92611, and holding that the statute was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant labor councils, on behalf of labor organizations representing employees of respondent college regents, sought a mandamus compelling respondents to comply with the statutory requirements establishing minimum salary limits for the representative class, as set out in Cal. Educ. Code § 92611. The trial court sustained respondents' demurrer without leave to amend, finding the statute unconstitutional under Cal. Const. art. IX, § 9. In reversing, the court held that its analysis revealed no infirmity in the statute or any irreconcilable conflict with the relevant constitutional provisions. The court found that in areas concerning a subject matter of statewide concern, the legislature had plenary power to adopt general laws promoting the public welfare that were binding upon respondents. Further, a legislatively declared purpose to preempt a field was entitled to great weight, and regulation in the field of labor relations was within the exclusive province of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The judgment sustaining respondent college regents general demurrer was reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with the opinion because the prevailing wage statute sought to be enforced by appellant labor councils was not in fatal conflict with the state constitution. The court upheld the validity of the statute.

 

1874 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Gillett-Harris-Duranceau & Associates, Inc. v. Kemple


Civ. No. 40857


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


83 Cal. App. 3d 214; 147 Cal. Rptr. 616; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1756



July 27, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Lake County, No. 13430, Frank S. Petersen, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff engineering firm sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Lake County (California), which entered a judgment of dismissal after a demurrer to plaintiff's second amended complaint was sustained without leave to amend in plaintiff's action against defendants, grand jury members, for defamation and interference with prospective business advantage.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, grand jury members, filed a public report that contained the statement that plaintiff engineering firm had been negligent, incompetent, and wrong in the performance of its duties. Plaintiff filed an action for defamation and interference with prospective business advantage. The trial court sustained the demurrer without leave to amend and concluded that a grand jury was a judicial body and was entitled to full immunity. The court reversed the dismissal and held that Cal. Penal Code § 930 applied both to grand jury reports on county officers and reports on special districts. Section 930 stated that if any grand jury commented upon any person or official who had not been indicted by the grand jury, such comments were not deemed to be privileged. The court found that although the sections preceding § 930 only mentioned reports on county officers and not reports concerning special districts, it was difficult to conceive of any legislative objective that would have been furthered by eliminating the privilege in connection with county officers but not special districts within the county. The imposed restrictions did not violate the California Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court in favor of defendants, grand jury members, because plaintiff engineering firm was not indicted, and such comments in the report concerning special districts were not deemed to be privileged.

 

1875 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Seatrain Terminals of Cal. v. County of Alameda


Civ. No. 39411


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


83 Cal. App. 3d 69; 147 Cal. Rptr. 578; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1742



July 24, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 23, 1978, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 20, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 465013-3, John P. Sparrow, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which held in favor of respondent in appellant's suit against respondent for recovery of real property taxes levied upon a possessory interest in two large cargo cranes owned by respondent and leased to appellant.


OVERVIEW: Respondent sold its marine facility to appellant. Respondent also leased two large cranes located on that property to respondent. Appellant then sued respondent for refund of taxes levied on its possessory interest in the cranes. The trial court determined that the cranes were fixtures and denied appellant's claim. Appellant sought the court's review. The court held that in determining whether an article constituted a fixture, three criteria were considered: the manner of its annexation to the realty, its adaptability to the use and purpose for which the realty was used, and the intention with which the annexation was made. Intent was the overriding factor, and was manifested by the physical facts. The facts here demonstrated that the cranes were extremely heavy, that they comprised a necessary, and working part of the rails attached to the property, and that without the cranes, the rails would lose their significance, and the property would not function in accordance with its purpose and design. Therefore, the cranes were properly considered constructively attached to the realty, and fully adapted to its use, and as a consequence, fixtures thereof. Judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Judgment in respondent's favor was affirmed where the trial court properly determined that the cranes at issue were fixtures on appellant's property, and that as a result, appellant was not entitled to a refund of taxes assessed on those cranes and paid by appellant.

 

1876 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Ward


Crim. No. 17736


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


82 Cal. App. 3d 981; 147 Cal. Rptr. 476; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1735



July 20, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 12, 1978. Mosk, J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of habeas corpus to annul a sentence for contempt of court imposed for violation of a preliminary injunction.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was a defendant in an action to enjoin the exhibition of an allegedly obscene motion picture. A preliminary injunction was granted based on the affidavit of a police officer that described the motion picture's content. Petitioner was found to be in violation of the injunction and was sentenced for contempt of court. Petitioner brought a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the validity of the injunction. Petitioner argued that the trial court did not conduct a "full adversary hearing" on the issue whether the film was obscene and that the trial court did not view the film for the purpose of resolving this issue. The court held the law did not prohibit a preliminary injunction pending a final determination on the merits of the obscenity issue. The court reasoned that the law only required that a prompt final judicial decision be available and any final adjudication be the result of a full adversary hearing. Moreover, the court held that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527(a) provided that a preliminary injunction could be issued on the basis of affidavits alone.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of habeas corpus and held that the trial court properly granted the preliminary injunction. The court reasoned that a full adversary hearing on the obscenity issue was not necessary to grant the injunction and that the trial court properly relied on a police officer's affidavit in granting the injunction.

 

1877 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Orosco


Crim. No. 30390


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


82 Cal. App. 3d 924; 147 Cal. Rptr. 463; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1730



July 19, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The order to show cause is discharged. The petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The California Supreme Court issued an order to show cause returnable before the court in petitioner's challenge to the constitutionality of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11550 through the request for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was sentenced to serve one year in county jail, the maximum sentence under the statute, following her conviction of violating Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11550. Petitioner contended that both the one-year maximum sentence and the mandatory minimum sentence of 90 days in jail called for by the statute violated the constitutional prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment. The court held that the arrest report submitted indicated that petitioner had track marks on her arm, old scar tissue, and six recent puncture wounds. Based on this limited record, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in imposing the maximum penalty. The court further found that petitioner's reliance on Cal. Veh. Code § 23105, which regulated driving under the influence, was not justified because § 23105 was part of a scheme regulating vehicular traffic, not drug traffic. Furthermore, the law was reasonable in light of other states which had chosen to penalize illicit drug use. The court held that § 11550 did not violate her equal protection rights even though certain drugs were not included in the statute because the state was not required to strike all evils at the same time.


OUTCOME: The petition for habeas corpus was denied and the order to show cause was discharged because, based on the evidence, the maximum sentenced imposed was not cruel or unusual punishment. Furthermore, petitioner's equal protection rights were not violated when because the state was not required to strike at all evils at the same time or in the same way.

 

1878 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Parish v. Lloyd


Civ. No. 16092


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


82 Cal. App. 3d 785; 147 Cal. Rptr. 431; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1718



July 13, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 370804, Douglas R. Woodworth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, family members of deceased, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) granting respondent property owner summary judgment, based on Cal. Civ. Code § 846, in appellants' wrongful death action against respondent resulting from an accident on respondent's property.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, family members of deceased, brought an action against respondent property owner for wrongful death, following an accident on respondent's property wherein the deceased was killed while riding his motorcycle. The trial court granted respondent summary judgment under Cal. Civ. Code § 846, which exempted property owners from liability for motorcyclists injured while trespassing on the property. The court affirmed the order of the trial court. In so ruling, the court rejected appellants' argument that § 846 violated U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found that the distinction between trespassers and paying invitees served the legitimate state purpose of encouraging property owners to grant recreational access to their property. Moreover, the court held that the state did not have to extend this protection against all possible users in order to achieve this legitimate interest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting respondent property owner summary judgment because the statute exempting property owners from liability for motorcycle injures sustained by trespassers did not violate the decedent's right to equal protection.

 

1879 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Socialist Labor Party v. Glendale


Civ. No. 52122.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


82 Cal. App. 3d 722; 147 Cal. Rptr. 422; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1714; 4 Media L. Rep. 1700



July 12, 1978


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Hg. den. October 4, 1978, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14  [**1] Cal. Rules of Ct., rule 976).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 130538, Robert I. Weil, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which granted summary judgment and preliminary injunction to respondent political party and held that Glendale, Cal., Code § 26-204, subd. i, which controlled newsrack placements, was unconstitutional in respondent's suit for declaratory relief and injunction.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city enacted Glendale, Cal., Code § 26-204, subd. i, which controlled placement of newsracks. Respondent political party, publisher of a weekly newspaper, sued appellant for declaratory relief and injunction, claiming law violated freedom of press, under U.S. Const. amend. I and Cal. Const. § 2, art. I, and equal protection, under U.S. Const. amends. I and XI and Cal. Const. § 7, art. I, by discriminating against weeklies in favor of dailies. When the trial court granted summary judgment and a preliminary injunction to respondent and held that the law was unconstitutional, appellant sought review. The court reversed. The ordinance was reasonable and constitutional on its face, there was no invidious discrimination against any particular class of newspaper and specifically none against respondent, and there was no unequal protection of the law as the ordinance was applied to respondent. The trial court simply granted summary judgment to respondent on the basis that the statute was unconstitutional. An issue of fact remained as to whether or not the ordinance favored the dailies to the detriment or actual exclusion of the weeklies.


OUTCOME: The court reversed preliminary injunction because there was no invidious discrimination against any particular class of newspaper and no unequal protection of the law as ordinance applied to respondent. The court reversed summary judgment for respondent because fact issue remained whether ordinance favored dailies to detriment or exclusion of weeklies.

 

1880 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pipkin v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 16995


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


82 Cal. App. 3d 652; 147 Cal. Rptr. 502; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1709



July 10, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 55789, Clyde H. Small, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and remanded to the trial court with directions that the Shasta County Employee Appeals Board be ordered to compute and award plaintiff accrued salary in accord with this opinion. The judgment is otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff deputy sheriff appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Shasta County (California), which denied a writ of mandate when defendant, the Shasta County Employee Appeals Board, refused to order plaintiff's case after she was dismissed as a matron of a jail for rudeness and insubordination.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff deputy sheriff was dismissed as a matron of a jail for rudeness and insubordination. Subsequently, defendant board refused to order her case and the trial court denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate filed in response. Plaintiff appealed. The court reversed a salary determination and remanded to the trial court to award plaintiff her accrued salary because plaintiff was not given an opportunity to explain the charges. However, the court affirmed plaintiff's dismissal because defendant's decision was supported by substantial evidence. The court ruled that plaintiff was not deprived of her due process rights. The evidence in support of the charge that she gave false reasons for absence from duty was supplied by her own admission and was confirmed by her husband's testimony so that defendant's finding on that charge was supported by substantial evidence. The finding that plaintiff was rude and insulting, used profanity, and was insubordinate was established by the testimony of fellow employees who witnessed the incidents and by her admission that she engaged in a personal dispute and used profanity with another deputy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a writ of mandate to command defendant board to order plaintiff deputy sheriff's case for wrongful termination because the decision was supported by substantial evidence. However, the court reversed the salary determination and remanded for a computation of plaintiff's accrued salary because plaintiff was not given an opportunity to explain the charges against her.

 

1881 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Christopher B.


Civ. No. 16886


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


82 Cal. App. 3d 608; 147 Cal. Rptr. 390; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1705



July 7, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 49509, 49510, William M. Gallagher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, mother and father, appealed the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that declared defendants' two children to be dependent children of the court and released them to the custody of defendants.


OVERVIEW: Defendant mother granted a sheriff, who was sent to investigate a child abuse complaint, permission to enter the house. While in the house, the sheriff observed deplorable conditions. When defendant mother refused homemaking assistance, the sheriff informed defendant mother that the children would be removed. Defendant mother resisted, resulting in the sheriff being forced to leave the house until assistance arrived. The trial court declared the children to be dependant children of the court and released them to into defendants' care, and the court affirmed. The court held that the exclusionary rule in U.S. Const. amend IV was inapplicable in civil dependency proceedings under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 300. Further, the court noted that defendant mother consented to the sheriff's initial search. The court also held that the proper standard of proof in § 300 cases where the child was not removed from parental custody was a preponderance of the evidence pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 355. The court held that this case contained substantial evidence to support the trial court's dependency order using the preponderance of the evidence standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court declaring the children of defendants, mother and father, to be dependent children of the court and releasing them into defendants' care. The court held that the warrantless search exclusionary rule did not apply to dependency proceedings and that preponderance of the evidence was the proper standard of proof in dependency proceedings.

 

1882 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hetherington v. State Personnel Bd.


Civ. No. 16513


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


82 Cal. App. 3d 582; 147 Cal. Rptr. 300; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1704; 27 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1182; 17 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P8606; 28 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P32,617



July 7, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 1, 1978, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 2, 1978. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein. Kaus, J., * participated therein. Mosk, J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by Acting Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 262124, Fred W. Marler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-felons challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) which denied appellants' request for injunctive and declaratory relief from appellee State Personnel Board's disqualifications of appellants from obtaining employment in any governmental positions classified as peace officers pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 1029.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-felons sought injunctive and declaratory relief from appellee State Personnel Board's disqualification of appellants from any governmental positions as peace officers pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 1029. The trial court denied appellants' petitions for relief and appellants' challenged the judgment of the trial court and the constitutionality of § 1029. The court held that § 1029 was not unconstitutional or violative of any equal protection clauses. More specifically, the court held that ex-felons were not a "suspect classification" nor did the occupations encompassed by § 1029 constitute fundamental interests to which a constitutional guarantee attached. The court also held that the statute bore a reasonable relationship to the legitimate state purpose of avoiding any appearance to the public that persons holding public positions were in any way untrustworthy. The court also found that § 1029 was not violative of due process of law on its face or in its application nor was it violative of federal and state guarantees of equal employment opportunities because its disqualification of ex-felons was not unconnected to the public expectation of peace officers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and determined that the statute prohibiting the employment of ex-felons in any governmental positions as peace officers was constitutional and that the statute passed both "strict scrutiny" and "reasonable basis" standards. The court also found that appellants' rights to due process were not violated by the statute, nor were their rights to equal employment opportunities.

 

1883 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cheatham


Crim. No. 31917.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


82 Cal. App. 3d 536; 147 Cal. Rptr. 141; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1699



July 3, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 24, 1978 (see 23 C.3d 829)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 076312, Raymond Choate, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that convicted appellant of first degree burglary. The trial court imposed the upper term limit for burglary based on aggravating factors and imposed three additional one-year sentences (staying one) based upon three prior convictions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree burglary after stealing a television from a retirement complex. At sentencing, the trial court imposed the upper limit sentence for burglary, finding aggravating factors such as appellant's parole, appellant's prior crimes as an adult that became more serious, and appellant's occasional use of heroin. The trial court then added three one-year sentences as enhancement for three prior convictions and stayed the enhancement of one term that was served concurrently with another crime. The court affirmed the judgment and sentence. The court held that under the Determinate Sentencing Act, Cal. Penal Code §1170.3, and Cal. R. Ct. 42, the trial court was permitted to consider factors that related to appellant's overall behavior and not just to the crime. The court held that the factors considered for aggravation and the prior convictions considered for sentence enhancement differed, as the trial court used the fact that appellant was on parole for the aggravation and used the prior convictions for enhancement. The court held that the additional enhancement terms did not violate appellant's guarantee against double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and sentence imposed by the trial court upon appellant's conviction for first degree robbery. The trial court was allowed to consider appellant's overall behavior for imposition of an aggravated sentence, and those considerations differed from the ones the court used for sentence enhancement.

 

1884 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Consumers Union of U. S., Inc. v. California Milk Producers Advisory Bd.


Civ. No. 42225


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


82 Cal. App. 3d 433; 147 Cal. Rptr. 265; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1689



July 3, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 20, 1978. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 705856, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Regulation 18703, subdivisions (c) and (d) are valid and the trial court is ordered to enter judgment consistent with the views expressed herein. Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, state commission, advisory board, and affiliated officials, appealed the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which granted a writ of mandate in favor of plaintiff public interest groups striking down Cal. Code Regs. tit. 2, § 18703 as violative of the Political Reform Act of 1974.


OVERVIEW: After passage of the Political Reform Act of 1974 (Act), implementing regulations provided that a person selected to serve on a state board or commission could be drawn from the trade or profession which the board affects. Plaintiff public interest groups won a writ of mandate in the superior court challenging the regulation, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 2, § 18703, as violative of the Act. The court reversed, concluding in favor of defendants, state commission, advisory board, and affiliated officials, that § 18703 properly advanced the intent of the Act, and that the Act was not meant to abrogate the state boards and commissions that drew on interested individuals for official service. The regulation's definition of "public generally" comported with the intent of the Act, and was impliedly ratified by the legislature. Further, conflicts experienced by board members could be addressed through measures, such as abstention, provided in the regulations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the writ of mandate which issued in favor of plaintiff public interest groups, concluding that the Political Reform Act of 1974 did not preclude members of state oversight boards or commissions from being drawn from the same industry, trade, or profession that they affect. Finding in favor of defendants, state commission, advisory board and affiliated officials, the court upheld the regulation defining "public generally."

California Hotel & Motel Asso. v. Industrial Welfare Com.


Civ. No. 17148.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


82 Cal. App. 3d 346; 146 Cal. Rptr. 909; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1683; 84 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,141



June 29, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 25, 1978 (see 25 C.3d 200)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Nevada County, No. 21625, Harold F. Wolters, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hotel association appealed an order of the Nevada County Superior Court (California), that denied its petition for a writ of mandate to enjoin defendant agency from enforcing an order establishing wages, hours, and working conditions for public housekeepers.


OVERVIEW: Defendant agency issued an order that established wages, hours, and working conditions for public housekeepers. Plaintiff hotel association filed a petition for a writ of mandate to preclude defendant from enforcing its order. Plaintiff appealed the denial of its petition. The court held that defendant's order did not contain an adequate statement of basis as required by Cal. Lab. Code § 1177, as it did not explain the reason for its order, but merely recited statutory authority for the order. The order denying plaintiff's petition was therefore reversed, and the case was remanded with instructions for issuance of plaintiff's writ of mandate and of an order compelling defendant to vacate its order. The court rejected plaintiff's contentions that defendant failed to investigate the industry or consider wage board recommendations before issuing its order as required by Cal. Lab. Code §1178, that defendant's order caused undue hardship in the industry, that defendant's order did not contain full findings regarding its economic impact, and that defendant acted arbitrarily, capriciously, and unconstitutionally in promulgating its order.


OUTCOME: Order denying plaintiff hotel association's petition for a writ of mandate was reversed because defendant agency's order did not contain an adequate statement of basis, as required by statute; the case was remanded for issuance of plaintiff's writ of mandate and an order compelling defendant to vacate its order.

 

1886 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


California Teachers Assn. v. Board of Trustees


Civ. No. 19281


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


82 Cal. App. 3d 249; 146 Cal. Rptr. 850; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1671



June 28, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 24, 1978. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 269617, Harmon G. Scoville, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teachers association appealed a judgment on the pleadings by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which upheld the constitutionality of education statutes that authorized defendant school board to enter into contracts with approved private driver training schools to provide automobile driver training.


OVERVIEW: Defendant school board entered into contracts with private schools to provide driver training instruction to pupils of the district as authorized by Cal. Educ. Code § 41913. Plaintiff teachers association sought an injunction, claiming that statutes like § 41913 authorized the transfer of a part of the public school system in violation of Cal. Const. art. IX, § 6 and authorized the appropriation of public money for the support of schools not under the exclusive control of the officers of the public schools in violation of art. IX, § 8. The trial court entered judgment on the pleadings upholding the constitutionality of §§ 41913- 41919, and the validity of agreements executed pursuant thereto. Plaintiffs appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the state legislature's delegation of the teaching function did not constitute a transfer of a part of the public school system. Nor did the statutes authorize the appropriation or expenditure of public funds in support of any school not under the exclusive control of the officers of the public schools. Therefore, the state constitution was not violated by the statutes relied upon by defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment on the pleadings for defendant school board, holding that statutes authorizing school districts to enter into contracts with approved private driver training schools to provide automobile driver training did not violate the state constitutional prohibitions against the transfer of a part of the public school system and the appropriation of public money for the support of private schools.

Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District

5 The motion of CVS filed March 21, 1977, to take judicial notice of the "Notice of Intended Decision" of Judge Schreck in Sacramento Superior Court case No. 211535 has been under submission. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state board of optometry and real party in interest nonprofit corporation sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate and declaratory relief in favor of respondent optometrist that commanded the board to compel the nonprofit corporation to alter its criteria for membership.

People v. Lucas


Crim. No. 31889


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


82 Cal. App. 3d 47; 147 Cal. Rptr. 235; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1651



June 22, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 22, 1978. On July 21, 1978, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Hearing denied September 14, 1978.

* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three appeals by appellant recipients of traffic citations were transferred from the Los Angeles Superior Court (California), pursuant to Cal. Court. R. 64, to settle the question of the constitutionality of Cal. Gov't Code § 72190.


OVERVIEW: Appellant recipients of traffic citations were found guilty of various traffic infractions. 1889 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Theresa Enterprises, Inc. v. Davis


Civ. Nos. 51830, 51831


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


81 Cal. App. 3d 940; 146 Cal. Rptr. 802; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1638



June 20, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On July 17, 1978, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondents' petitions for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied August 24, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C 201494, C 200856, Charles H. Phillips, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the trial court granting the preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which enjoined defendants and their agents from arresting or citing plaintiffs, or in any manner causing plaintiffs to be arrested or cited under or for violation of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code §§ 42.21 through 42.32, inclusive.


OVERVIEW: The trial court enjoined defendants and their agents from arresting or citing plaintiffs, or in any manner causing plaintiffs to be arrested or cited under or for violation of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code §§ 42.21 through 42.32, inclusive. Defendants claimed that plaintiffs' establishment, where the waitresses performed topless and nude dances, was not a "theater." On appeal, the court agreed with defendants. Merely because an establishment had a stage, fixed seats, and entertainment did not make that establishment a "theater." Situations provided a rational basis to treat differently nude performers in theaters, on the one hand, and nude performers, waiters, and waitresses in establishments such as plaintiffs' on the other. It was obvious that the sale of food and beverage, encouraged and promoted by the presentation of nudity for the sake of sexual titillation, rather than the rendition of any First Amendment-protected entertainment, was the primary purpose of plaintiffs' establishment. The court reversed the order of the trial court granting the preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which enjoined defendants arresting plaintiffs under an ordinance prohibiting waitresses from serving food in the nude. Plaintiffs' establishment was not a "theater," and therefore was not protected by the First Amendment.

People v. Cave


Crim. No. 3052


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


81 Cal. App. 3d 957; 147 Cal. Rptr. 371; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1640



June 20, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 29, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 139854, Frank S. Pierson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California), which convicted defendant of a drug crime after denying his motion to dismiss the information on the grounds that he was denied his Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 right to a speedy trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged by information with the sale of heroin. The trial court denied defendant's motion to dismiss the information on the basis that he was denied his Cal. Const. art. I, § 15 right to a speedy trial, finding no unreasonable delay by the state. Defendant was found guilty and appealed. The reviewing court reversed the conviction, holding that defendant was denied his right to a speedy trial. The court found that defendant was clearly prejudiced by the delay and the procedure of holding up service of an arrest warrant until defendant returned to the jurisdiction was not a legitimate justification. The court found that defendant came within the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1381 but he did not have the opportunity to make the 90-day demand under the statute because the state made no effort to notify him of the pending offense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of conviction. The court held that defendant was denied his right to a speedy trial.

 

1891 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kaufman v. Gross & Co.


Civ. No. 52387.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


81 Cal. App. 3d 734; 146 Cal. Rptr. 706; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1617



June 8, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 31, 1978 (see 23 C.3d 750)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 187060, Jack T. Ryburn, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, grantee under a grant deed, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed his declaratory judgment action to quiet title as to property which he owned pursuant to a grant deed executed by the fee owner dated October 1975. Defendants, grantees under a treasurer's deed, claimed the same property pursuant to a deed executed by the Treasurer of the City of Los Angeles in December 1975.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, grantee under a grant deed, claimed property pursuant to a grant deed executed by the fee title owner. Defendants, grantees under a treasurer's deed, claimed the same property under a deed from the city dated three months later. The latter deed resulted from the foreclosure of a street lighting assessment lien under the Street Improvement Act of 1911, Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 5000 et seq. Plaintiff and his predecessor did not receive notice of the foreclosure because the city sent notices to a prior owner who was dead. Plaintiff filed a declaratory judgment action, claiming that the treasurer's deed was void for constitutionally deficient notice, and that therefore, the statute of limitations did not bar the suit. The trial court dismissed the action. The court reversed and held that plaintiff's allegations of defects in the tax proceedings were jurisdictional, and therefore sustained the complaint under Cal. Civ. Code § 3412. The court also held that the procedures outlined in Cal. Sts. & High. Code §§ 6501, 6502, and 6503 were constitutional, but that the city's failure to ascertain the real owner of the property before selling the property was a denial of due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment dismissing the declaratory judgment action of plaintiff, grantee under a grant deed, to quiet title to the property which defendants, grantees under treasurer 's deed, claimed because the city treasurer did not follow the proper procedures in sending statutory notices of tax delinquencies. The court remanded the case with directions for the trial court to overrule defendants' demurrers.

People v. Sand


Crim. No. 29955


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


81 Cal. App. 3d 448; 146 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1592



May 30, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 20, 1978. Jefferson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 27, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-131478, Armand Arabian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide for imprisonment for life on count I; the sentences on the other two counts are merged into that sentence ( Pen. Code, § 669); otherwise the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), convicting him of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187, robbery under Cal. Penal Code § 211, burglary under Cal. Penal Code § 459, murder during the commission and attempted commission of a robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211, and murder during the commission and attempted commission of a burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 460(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions for murder, robbery, and burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 187, 211, 459, respectively. The jury found true that defendant committed murder during the commission and attempted commission of a robbery in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 211 and during the commission and attempted commission of a burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 460(1). Defendant received a death sentence on the murder conviction, and the robbery and burglary sentences were to run concurrently with each other. The court affirmed the judgments of conviction as modified to provide for life imprisonment for murder, and the robbery and burglary sentences were merged into the murder sentence pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 669. The court rejected defendant's argument that the exclusion, at the guilt stage, of jurors opposed to the death penalty, resulted in a jury not representative of the community as a whole and in a jury overly prone to convict. The court concluded that defendant's conduct in the hospital was not prejudicial beyond any reasonable doubt. The provision under which death sentence was imposed had been held to be unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, defendant's convictions for murder, robbery, burglary, the commission of murder during the commission, and attempted commission of a robbery and burglary, and the robbery and burglary sentences were merged into the murder sentence because the death sentence had been held to be unconstitutional.

 

1893 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. McKellar


Crim. No. 30376.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


81 Cal. App. 3d 367; 146 Cal. Rptr. 327; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1585



May 26, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 20, 1978 (Cr. 20594)
Jan. 24, 1980, cause retrans to Ct. of App., 2d Dist., Div. 5, for refiling its opinion with appropriate reference to People v. Blair (1979) 2S Cal.3d 640, 653-654.
Opn. filed Feb. 11, 1980, not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 074470, Laurence J. Rittenband, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant male was convicted in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of unlawful sexual intercourse with a female under 18 years of age who was not the perpetrator's wife, Cal. Penal Code § 261.5. Appellant sought reversal, arguing that the statute could not survive strict constitutional scrutiny, and violated his right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I. § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant male was charged with unlawful sexual intercourse, Cal. Penal Code § 261.5 and oral copulation, Cal. Penal Code § 288a, with a 16-year old girl. Both parties consented to the sexual activity. Appellant pled guilty to misdemeanor violation of § 261.5. Appellant sought reversal, challenging the constitutionality of § 261.5, which proscribed sexual intercourse with a female under 18 years of age who was not the perpetrator's wife. The court affirmed, holding that § 261.5 was a proper exercise of the legislature's police power and that the classification was reasonable and bore a genuine and substantial relationship to the object of the legislature. The court found that the sexes were not similarly situated to the state's interest in preventing pregnancy in females under 18 years of age, as females could become pregnant and males could not, males could not suffer as seriously as females the adverse medical, social, and psychological consequences of teenage pregnancy. The court held that the statute did not violate appellant's right to privacy under Cal. Const. art. I, § I, as applied to consensual conduct, because the issue did not involve approved consensual conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant male's conviction for unlawful sexual intercourse with a female under 18 years of age who was not his wife. The court held that the statute did not require equal protection as the sexes were not similarly situated with respect to the legislature's goal of preventing teenage pregnancy and that the statute did not violate appellant's constitutional right to privacy.

 

1894 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


M Restaurants, Inc. v. San Francisco Local Joint Executive Board of Culinary Workers, etc.


Civ. No. 40108.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


81 Cal. App. 3d 203; 146 Cal. Rptr. 436; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1571; 98 L.R.R.M. 2912; 99 L.R.R.M. 2741



May 25, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] as modified June 15, 1978
Hg. granted Aug. 3, 1978 (S.F. 23889)
Jan. 24, 1980, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 2, with directions to refile its opinion with appropriate discussion of Kaplan's Fruit & Produce Co. v. Superior Court (1979) 26 Cal.3d 60.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 684895, Victor M. Campilongo, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant union sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied appellant's motion to dissolve the preliminary injunction granted to appellee restaurant enjoining appellant union from threatening and/or committing acts of intimidation and violence and limiting the numbers and spacing of picketers where it was issued prior to the Moscone Bill, Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3.


OVERVIEW: Appellee restaurant engaged in conduct to discourage appellant union's efforts after the employees voted the union in. Appellant engaged in activity, such as picketing, which led appellee to seek injunctive relief to prevent appellant from engaging in improper or illegal picketing of its premises. The trial court granted a preliminary injunction enjoining appellant from blocking access or deliveries to the restaurant, threatening and/or committing acts of intimidation and physical violence on anyone working for or desiring to do business with appellee restaurant, and disturbing the peace. Appellant sought review of a decision denying a motion to dissolve based on a retroactive application of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3. The court affirmed the denial of the motion, holding that the preliminary injunction was a decree continuing in nature directed at future events and that § 527.3 did apply. The court held that nothing in § 527.3(b) prevented a court from placing reasonable restrictions on picketing where the absence of such restrictions had resulted in threats of violence and that under § 527.3 the courts needed not wait until actual violence erupted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court upholding the preliminary injunction granted appellee restaurant because the courts were not required to wait until actual violence erupted and the court could properly enjoin violence, threats of violence and obstructions to ingress and egress which threatened public health and safety.

 

1895 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pan Pacific Properties, Inc. v. County of Santa Cruz


Civ. No. 41428


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


81 Cal. App. 3d 244; 146 Cal. Rptr. 428; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1574



May 25, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 60272, Harry F. Brauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment and order denying motion to tax costs are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowners challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which dismissed appellants' action for inverse condemnation and declaratory relief, following its order sustaining respondent county's demurrer, arising out of the rezoning of appellants' land.


OVERVIEW: Respondent county rezoned appellant landowners' land from agricultural to agricultural with a one-acre minimum building site. Appellants brought an action for inverse condemnation and declaratory relief, alleging that the rezoning was arbitrary and discriminatory, and that it constituted a taking because it prohibited all beneficial and reasonable use. The trial court sustained respondent's demurrer with prejudice, and then dismissed the action. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that appellants' failure to exhaust their administrative remedies, by applying for a variance, before pursuing their lawsuit was a jurisdictional defect. The court found that appellants' refusal to apply for a zoning variance or a building or use permit foreclosed any action attacking the validity of the zoning ordinance, finding that it was not certain that they would have received an adverse decision on any such application. The court also held that the rezoning did not constitute a "taking" for eminent domain purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal, holding that appellants' failure to exhaust their administrative remedies before pursuing their inverse condemnation lawsuit was a jurisdictional defect, and finding that it was not certain that they would have received an adverse decision had they applied for a variance or building permit.

In re Marriage of Roesch

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


81 Cal. App. 3d 117; 146 Cal. Rptr. 255; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1563

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 451872-7, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant ex-wife challenged the amount of spousal support, the conditional nature of child support, and the division of property ordered by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which dissolved her marriage to respondent ex-husband.


OVERVIEW: Appellant ex-wife challenged the decree of divorce entered by the trial court. Spousal support and child support were properly charged to respondent's separate income. The court modified the judgment to delete the provision that made child support conditional and affirmed the judgment as modified.


OUTCOME: The court deleted the provision that made child support conditional and affirmed the judgment as modified because the court abused its discretion when it made child support conditional on lack of interference with respondent ex-husband's visitation rights.

Miller v. Superior Court of Alameda County

OVERVIEW: A complaint was filed in municipal court that alleged defendant had violated Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), assault with a deadly weapon. Defendant pled not guilty at the preliminary hearing and an information was filed in the trial court. Defendant was arraigned and physicians were appointed to examine defendant. The trial court found that defendant was mentally incompetent to stand trial and committed him to the state hospital. Defendant was released from the hospital and found competent to stand trial. On review the court issued the writ of mandate and held that because defendant was found mentally incompetent shortly after the trial court arraignment he was incompetent to cooperate with his counsel and was not lawfully committed to stand trial and the information had to be set aside.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's writ of mandate and ordered the trial court to set aside the information that charged him with assault with a deadly weapon or force because defendant was not lawfully committed to stand trial.

Shea v. Board of Medical Examiners


Civ. No. 15466


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


81 Cal. App. 3d 564; 146 Cal. Rptr. 653; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1602



May 19, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 14, 1978, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 19, 1978. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 253061, Charles W. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed. The stay heretofore issued is dissolved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant doctor sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied his application for a writ of mandate directed to respondent Board of Medical Examiners. Respondent had found appellant guilty of unprofessional conduct within the meaning of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 1094.5. Appellant contended that Cal. Bus. & Prof. § 2361, was vague and indefinite and that he was not accorded proper notice.


OVERVIEW: Appellant doctor was alleged to have attempted to hypnotize four patients and while believing them to be under hypnosis described to them in lurid and salacious detail sexual foreplay and the act of sexual intercourse. At an administrative hearing on the charges, the hearing officer recommended six month's suspension. Respondent Board of Medical Examiners agreed with the decision, except for the penalty. The board held that appellant had to submit to a psychiatric examination for determination of his emotional ability to practice medicine and on resumption of practice, he was to be on probation for a five-year period. Appellant contended, along with other issues, that Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2361 was vague and indefinite, the penalty was excessive, and respondent board did not file its decision timely. The court held that § 2361 was not unconstitutionally vague or indefinite, the state had broad powers to regulate who practiced medicine within the state, and there was no statute which expressly limited the time within which a board had to issue its' decision. The court held that respondent did not abuse its' discretion. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court upholding a decision of the Board of Medical Examiners was affirmed, because appellant doctor's sexual statements to his patients when he believed them to be hypnotized was unprofessional conduct and the state had the obligation and power to protect its citizens by the regulation of the professional conduct of its' health practitioners.

San Diego Gas & Electric Co. v. San Diego


Civ. No. 16277.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


80 Cal. App. 3d 1026; 146 Cal. Rptr. 103; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1486



May 17, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 13, 1978 (L.A. 30985)
Reprinted as modified on den. rehg. 81 Cal. App. 3d 844. Opn. filed June 26, 1979, not for publication


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 357022, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found that defendant had inversely condemned property owned by plaintiff company and which awarded damages for the value of the property, for severance, and for interest.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff company owned a parcel of land that was zoned for industrial and agricultural use. Defendant city downzoned some of plaintiff's land and adopted an open space plan that covered the land. Defendant's bond issue for acquisition of the land failed, and plaintiff filed suit. The court affirmed the trial court's finding that there was an inverse condemnation based on substantial evidence, which showed that defendant would deny any application for industrial development on the land because of the open space designation, that the land had no economic use, and that no one would buy it. Therefore, the court held that compensation for the value of the property was properly awarded, and it also affirmed the trial court's calculation of interest on the award from the date of the taking rather than from the date of the judgment. The award of severance damages was affirmed because the use of the condemned parcel was not dissimilar to that of the remainder parcel, and there was access between the two parcels. Other trial court findings and conclusions were affirmed based on substantial evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiff company because defendant city's designation of plaintiff's land as open space amounted to an inverse condemnation.

 

1900 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mills


Crim. No. 8674


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


81 Cal. App. 3d 171; 146 Cal. Rptr. 411; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1567



May 17, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 13, 1978. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 38697, Daniel C. Leedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of lewd and lascivious conduct on a child under Cal. Penal Code § 288. Defendant argued that his registration as a sex offender constituted cruel and unusual punishment; that it violated the equal protection clause; it impinged on his right to travel, his privacy, and infringed on his right of freedom of association and movement.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of lewd and lascivious conduct on a child and given probation. As a condition of probation, defendant was required to register as a convicted sex offender, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 290, and ordered not to have any contact with minors, nor frequent places where they congregated unless in he did so in the presence of responsible adults. On appeal, defendant argued that registration was cruel and unusual punishment; it violated the equal protection clause; it impinged on his right to travel and his privacy; and it infringed on this right to freedom of association and movement. The court affirmed the judgment and held that the registration requirement was not out of all proportion to the crime and was not cruel and unusual punishment. Moreover, defendant's equal protection claim was without merit, because the state had a compelling interest in protecting children and conviction for such a crime carried with it a reduction to some degree of defendant's rights to privacy, travel and freedom of association.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, holding that registration was not cruel and unusual punishment because it was not disproportionate to the crime committed. The court held further that commission of and conviction for a public offense resulted in certain restrictions on a perpetrator's rights to travel, privacy, and freedom to associate.

 

1901 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hughes v. Neth


Civ. No. 19514


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


80 Cal. App. 3d 952; 146 Cal. Rptr. 37; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1479



May 16, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 13, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 272893, Harmon G. Scoville, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant engine owner challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which entered judgment discharging an alternative writ and denying a peremptory writ sought by appellant to prevent the destruction or disposition of a motorcycle engine, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 10751(b), without notice or a hearing, because the vehicle identification numbers had been altered, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 10751(a).


OVERVIEW: Respondent police seized appellant engine owners' motorcycle because the vehicle identification numbers had been altered, and impounded the engine. Appellant filed an action for a peremptory writ to prevent the destruction or disposition of the motorcycle engine, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 10751(b), without notice or a hearing, because the vehicle identification numbers had been altered in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 10751(a). The trial court discharged the alternative writ that had been obtained and denied the peremptory writ. On appeal, the only issue was the constitutionality of § 10751(b), which provided for destruction or disposition, without notice and hearing, of vehicles or vehicle parts seized pursuant to § 10751(a). The court declared that § 10751(b), violated the due process requirement of Cal. Const. art. I, § 7 and U.S. Const. amend XIV because it failed to provide for a notice and hearing prior to forfeiture of property. Appellant had a constitutional right to the return of the property or a noticed hearing where she was given the opportunity to oppose respondent's intended forfeiture and destruction of it.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court order denying a peremptory writ to prevent the disposition or destruction of appellant engine owners' motorcycle engine because the statute permitting such action, without notice and an opportunity for hearing, violated the due process requirement of both state and federal constitutions, and there were no exigent circumstances which would warrant an opposite result.

 

1902 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Regional Center of Orange v. King


Civ. No. 19215


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


80 Cal. App. 3d 860; 146 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1468



May 11, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 177421, Charles Bierschbach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a developmentally disabled person, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) in favor of respondent state regional center, which denied appellant a jury trial upon request in a commitment proceeding pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 38009.1 and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 38009.2.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied appellant, a developmentally disabled person, a jury trial in a commitment proceeding. The trial court noted that there was no express statutory provision for jury trials in commitment proceedings under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 38009.1 and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 38009.2. The court found, however, that mentally retarded persons were nevertheless entitled to a jury trial upon request in commitment proceedings. The court noted that commitment proceedings could result in a substantial loss of liberty, and that the state violated equal protection if it granted jury trials to some groups and denied them to others unless the different treatment was necessary for a compelling state interest. The court reversed the trial court's decision, finding that respondent state regional center had failed to demonstrate a compelling state interest in denying a right to jury trial to developmentally disabled persons. Consequently, appellant, and other developmentally disabled persons, were entitled to a jury trial upon request in commitment proceedings pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 38009.1 and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 38009.2.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision denying appellant, a developmentally disabled person, a jury trial upon request in a commitment proceeding. Appellant was entitled to a jury trial, even though there was no express statutory provision, because a commitment proceeding could result in a substantial loss of liberty, and respondent state regional center failed to demonstrate a compelling state interest to deny such right.

1904 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


M. Lowenstein & Sons, Inc. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 16405


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


80 Cal. App. 3d 762; 145 Cal. Rptr. 814; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1457



May 9, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for writ of mandate is denied. The order to show cause and the order staying the proceedings in the superior court are discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation sought a writ of mandate and/or prohibition to overturn the denial by respondent trial court of petitioner's motion to quash service and alternatively to dismiss real party in interest individual's complaint.


OVERVIEW: Respondent trial court denied petitioner corporation's motion to quash service and alternatively to dismiss real party in interest individual's complaint. The court refused to grant a writ of mandate to overturn the trial court's order. The court concluded that although service of process could have properly been made on petitioner's designated agent by any of the methods prescribed for service within the state, the agent was not the only person upon whom service could be made. Among the statutory alternatives open to real party in interest was that of serving by mail a copy of the summons and complaint as provided by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 415.40. The court found that due process was not violated because § 415.40 provided petitioner with actual notice and an opportunity to be heard. The court also found that § 415.40 did not deny equal protection to petitioner because the non-residence of petitioner necessarily made for a distinctive procedure. The court concluded that a return receipt and a letter were satisfactory evidence of actual delivery to petitioner and that the return of service, as amended, operated to take the case out of the provisions of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 581a.


OUTCOME: The petition for writ of mandate to overturn respondent trial court's orders was denied. Among the statutory alternatives open to real party in interest individual was that of serving a copy of the summons and complaint on petitioner corporation by mail.
