Kaiser Foundation Hospitals v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 50512.Civ. No. 50671.Civ. No. 50798.Civ. No. 50921.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


80 Cal. App. 3d 787; 146 Cal. Rptr. 86; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1460



May 8, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted June 2, 1978 (see 87 C.A.3d 336)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hospital corporation challenged separate determinations of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California) and four individuals that were consolidated to address common problems interpreting Cal. Lab. Code § 4903.1. The rulings reduced the amount owing on appellant's lien for medical services provided. Appellant argued it was denied equal protection and due process in applying § 4903.1.


OVERVIEW: The court ruled in favor of appellant hospital corporation on its appeal of four consolidated determinations by respondents Workers' Compensation Board of Appeals and four individuals. Appellant challenged the board's reduction of amount owing on appellant's liens for medical services provided and argued that the board's application of Cal. Lab. Code § 4903.1(c) denied it due process and equal protection. The court applied the rational relationship test and concluded the statute served a legitimate state purpose to encourage compromises and settlements. Added legislative protection provided that settlements were scrutinized by the board to avoid unfairness. Legislative history demonstrated that § 4903.1(c) applied to liens where settlement was not reached prior to January 1, 1976. The court annulled respondent's decisions and remanded for further proceedings because respondent did not comply with due process requirements - by defects in serving notice, by lacking appropriate documentation in serving respondent, and in not providing specific data to reduce appellant's lien recovery.


OUTCOME: The court annulling respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board's decisions and remanding for further proceedings. The court found the statute had immediate effect and applied to liens where settlements were not reached prior to January 1, 1976. The court held that the statute was constitutional and applicable to the four cases and that respondent's procedures did not comply with due process requirements.

 

1906 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rocha


Crim. No. 16585


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


80 Cal. App. 3d 972; 146 Cal. Rptr. 81; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1481



May 5, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 63936, James A. Wright, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to stay execution of sentence imposed on count I, such stay to become permanent when defendant has completed serving the sentence imposed on count II. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which convicted defendant under Cal. Penal Code § 192, 3(a), and Cal. Veh. Code § 23101.


OVERVIEW: The court modified the judgment of the lower court to stay the execution of defendant's sentence for felony vehicular manslaughter under Cal. Penal Code § 192, 3(a). The court held that the stay would become permanent after defendant had completed his sentence for felony drunk driving under Cal. Veh. Code § 23101. The court found that there was substantial evidence to show that at the time of the injury that caused the other drivers death, defendant was driving under the influence of alcohol, driving at an excessive rate of speed, and had run a red light. The court held that such evidence supported the jury finding that defendant was unlawful and grossly negligent, or that a lawful act likely to produce death was committed by defendant. The court held that defendant had been improperly sentenced on both convictions and determined that in order to remedy the sentence, defendant was to serve the greater of the two sanctions. The court held the penalty under Cal. Veh. Code § 23101(a) was the greater sanction and that the conviction and sentence under § 23101(a) was to remain.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower court judgment to show that defendant's sentence for felony vehicular manslaughter was to be stayed because the sanction for the felony drunk driving conviction provided the greater sanction of the two convictions.

 

1907 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Henderson


Crim. No. 29951


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


80 Cal. App. 3d 584; 145 Cal. Rptr. 751; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1444



May 2, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 8, 1978. A petition for a rehearing was denied May 25, 1978, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 28, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. SM-16396, Arden T. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide a punishment of life imprisonment instead of death and, as so modified, is affirmed. 13



13 The People's submission that the case should be remanded for a new death penalty trial has no merit. ( People v. Harvey (1977) 76 Cal.App.3d 441 [142 Cal.Rptr. 887]; People v. Payne (1977) 75 Cal.App.3d 601 [142 Cal.Rptr. 320].)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought the review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which entered a jury verdict convicting defendant of first degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a). The death penalty was imposed.


OVERVIEW: After a jury returned a guilty verdict, defendant was convicted of first-degree murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a) and sentenced to death. On appeal, defendant argued that he had received ineffective counsel at trial and that the prosecution's death qualification of prospective jurors violated his due process rights. The court affirmed the guilty verdict, but modified defendant's sentence to life imprisonment holding that the trial counsel's failure to make certain objections may have been part of his defense tactic and did not amount to ineffective assistance. The court also held that it was proper for the prosecution to death qualify prospective jurors and that such practice did not violate defendant's rights to a jury that was made up of a cross-section of the community. Finally, the court held that because the prospective jurors were qualified based on § 190.2(a), which was later invalidated, the death sentence needed to have been modified.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's conviction of defendant for first degree murder, but modified the judgment to provide for life imprisonment instead of death; the statute under which defendant had been convicted was later found to have been invalid.

Lanam v. Civil Service Com.


Civ. No. 40791


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


80 Cal. App. 3d 315; 145 Cal. Rptr. 590; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1420



April 25, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 37781, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a decision from respondent Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which entered judgment for respondent employee, determining that he had been unlawfully dismissed because the dimensions of the residency area wherein respondent was required to live violated Cal. Const. art. XI, §10(b).


OVERVIEW: Respondent employee was dismissed from his position because he resided outside the required residency area. He brought an in administrative mandamus proceeding against appellant city. The trial court determined that he had been unlawfully dismissed because the dimensions of the residency area violated Cal. Const. art. XI, §10(b). Appellant sought review arguing that the legislative action which established its residence requirement, and the area involved, was attended by a strong presumption of validity. The court held that the legislative action was reasonable in itself, within the meaning of the permissive constitutional language, insofar as its purpose was to require appellant's public safety employees to live at places from which they could effectively be called to duty when they were needed. However, the mere specification of a distance in miles did not make it reasonable. Further, the court held that the trial court was the ultimate finder of fact, and that its decision was not error because it was clearly supported by evidence and stare decisis.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgment in favor of respondent employee because the mere specification of a distance in miles did not make appellant city's requirement that its employees live within a certain reasonable.

 

1909 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Flood v. Riggs


Civ. No. 40846


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


80 Cal. App. 3d 138; 145 Cal. Rptr. 573; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1405



April 21, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 16, 1978, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 15, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 478955-0, Robert K. Barber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant paroled felon sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his petition requesting that respondents, county registrar of voters, secretary of state, and adult authority, register him to vote, despite the uniform statewide policy that denied the franchise to all felons who had not completed their parole, pursuant to Cal. Const. art II, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Appellant paroled felon sought a writ of mandamus directing respondents, county registrar of voters, secretary of state, and adult authority, to register him as otherwise qualified to vote. Appellant challenged the validity of a uniform policy of blanket disfranchisement of paroled ex-felons pursuant to Cal. Const. art. II, § 4, contending such policy was neither constitutionally required nor authorized by the California Elections Code. The trial court denied appellant's petition. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the legislative history and purpose of the relevant provisions of the California Constitution and Elections Code permitted the state to deny a convicted felon the voting franchise during the term of his parole under the self-executing provisions of art. II, § 4. The court also held that the several implementing provisions of the Elections Code inconsistent with that constitutional mandate were invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which denied appellant paroled felon's petition to be registered to vote. The court held that the legislative history and purpose of the California Constitution permitted the state to deny the voting franchise to a convicted felon who had not yet served the term of his parole.

In re Secada

Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One

DISPOSITION:  [***1] The Director of the Department of Corrections is directed to instruct the Community Release Board to recompute Secada's term in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion and California Administrative Code, title 15, section 2145. The order to show cause is discharged and the writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus, contending that his parole eligibility date was miscalculated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted on four separate narcotics counts in the trial court. Defendant petitioned for a writ of habeas corpus, alleging that his parole eligibility date had been miscalculated. Defendant contended that under Cal. Penal Code § 3024, he was eligible for parole within 40 months. The prosecution asserted that Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 11360 and 11352 mandated longer terms of parole ineligibility and that those terms had to run consecutively, as the sentences did. The court reasoned that under the theory of the prosecution a defendant might not be eligible for parole after he had served his minimum sentence. Further, the court held that the imposition of consecutive parole ineligibility terms was unconstitutional. While the court denied the writ of habeas corpus, it ordered that the parole date be recalculated consistent with its opinion.


OUTCOME: The court denied the writ of habeas corpus but instructed that the parole eligibility date of defendant be recalculated because the imposition of consecutive parole ineligibility terms was unconstitutional.

 

1911 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Carroll


Crim. No. 17293


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


80 Cal. App. 3d 22; 145 Cal. Rptr. 334; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1396



April 19, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 16, 1978, and the petitions of both the parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied June 15, 1978. Clark, J., and Richardson, J., were of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought a habeas corpus petition after his parole was revoked by the Adult Authority under Cal. Penal Code § 290. The California Supreme Court issued an order to show cause. Defendant contended that he was denied due process because he was deprived of the right to confront and cross-examine adverse witnesses whom the Adult Authority had no power to subpoena.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was on parole when he was arrested for alleged breaking and entering and assault. When he was served with the notice of the Adult Authority's suspension of his parole, he denied the charges and requested a hearing at which he was represented by counsel. The arresting police officer and investigator testified at the hearing. The court affirmed the revocation of defendant's parole on the basis of the evidence, which was properly admitted under the board's more flexible evidentiary rules. The court held that the Adult Authority had authority and power to issue subpoenas pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 11181. The court ruled that the victim's disappearance provided good cause for her absence and that admission of her prior statements was proper because they were adequately corroborated. The court found that the non-appearance of a second witness was not prejudicial because he was not an adverse witness and deletion of his statement would not have vindicated defendant. The court found that the non-appearance of defendant's companion was not prejudicial because he was not shown to be an adverse witness and defendant could equally have secured his attendance.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for habeas corpus because the victim's disappearance furnished good cause for her absence and her prior statements were adequately corroborated, the absence of a second witness was not prejudicial because he was not an adverse witness, and the third witness, defendant's companion, was also available to defendant. The court held that the Adult Authority had subpoena power to call witnesses to its hearings.

 

1912 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mount Vernon Memorial Park v. Board of Funeral Directors & Embalmers


Civ. No. 16311


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


79 Cal. App. 3d 874; 145 Cal. Rptr. 275; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1558



April 14, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 8, 1978, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 15, 1978. Bird, C. J., and Manuel, J., did not Participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 250974, Robert W. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant memorial park sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which affirmed a decision of respondent board revoking appellant's funeral director's license.


OVERVIEW: Appellant memorial park possessed a dual license to act as a cemetery authority and mortuary. Respondent board filed a complaint alleging appellant's preneed funeral arrangement contracts with clients violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 7738, because appellant did not provide that the contract money would be placed in trust and returned upon written request and revocation of the agreement with clients. The trial court sustained appellee board's findings revoking appellant's funeral license and the court affirmed. The court held that § 7738 made it clear that the corpus of the trust could not be used to pay commissions or other trust expenses, and clarified that a licensed cemetery authority need not establish a separate trust for preeneed funeral arrangement funds. Therefore, the preeneed funeral contracts by appellant were not exempt from the requirements under § 7738 because the legislature did not seek to prohibit the potential abuses by funeral directors that happen to be dual licensees, such as appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment revoking appellant memorial park's funeral license, because the legislature did not intend to prohibit potential abuses of preneed funeral contracts by mortuaries, yet tolerate them by dual licensees such as appellant, who held a license as both a mortuary and a cemetery authority.

Board of Trustees v. Cory

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner private university sought a peremptory writ of mandate, which would compel respondents, state controller and state treasurer, to pay petitioner's claim for state funds for increased private medical school enrollment under a contract with the state.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner private university entered into a contract whereby the state was to pay funds to petitioner in return for increased enrollment in petitioner's medical school. In re P.


Civ. No. 51433


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION Three


79 Cal. App. 3d 416; 144 Cal. Rptr. 868; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1383



April 4, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 5, 1978 (see 25 C.3d 91)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. V 70, 45268 Peter S. Smith, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor appealed from an order of the juvenile court traffic division of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied her motion for a rehearing in her underlying traffic violation case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant minor was charged with a traffic violation, pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 21804. Her hearing was conducted by a traffic hearing officer upon a copy of the violation notice, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 257, and a fine was imposed. Defendant then sought to appeal the determination, but her request for rehearing was denied by the juvenile court traffic division on the findings of the hearing officer. Defendant appealed the denial of the rehearing. The court reversed the juvenile court's order, finding that defendant's constitutional rights to a hearing by a judge had been violated by the appointment of the traffic hearing officer to her case, without the express stipulation of defendant or her family. In addition, the record of the traffic hearing officer was insufficient and did not provide the court with sufficient information to reach an independent judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the juvenile court, denying defendant minor a rehearing in her traffic violation case, holding that the hearing by a traffic hearing officer violated defendant's constitutional rights to have her case heard by a judge. The record of the hearing officer was also insufficient to provide the court sufficient information to reach an independent judgment.

East Bay Municipal Utility Dist. v. Appellate Dep't of Superior Court


Civ. No. 42284.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


79 Cal. App. 3d 362; 144 Cal. Rptr. 845; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1519



Apr. 4, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 1, 1978 (see 23 C.3d 839)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner utility district brought a petition for writ of certiorari and/or mandamus against respondent, the Appellate Department of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which reversed petitioner's judgments against non-paying political candidates, against whom petitioner had won collection actions for money billed for campaign costs, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 10012.5.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner utility district billed political candidates for statutory costs, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 10012.5, involving creation of campaign material. When the candidates failed to pay, petitioner brought a collection action in municipal court and won judgments. On the candidates' appeal of the judgments, respondent appellate court reversed, holding that § 10012.5 could not constitutionally permit enforced collection of the costs. Petitioner sought a writ of certiorari or mandamus, to annul the decision of respondent. The court affirmed the decision, holding that respondent accurately interpreted § 10012.5 as providing municipal agencies the right to bill candidates, but not to enforce the collection of those bills. It reasoned that the opposite ruling would unconstitutionally discriminate against less affluent candidates, who were unable to pay the costs of the campaign.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed respondent appellate court's ruling, holding that petitioner utility district had no statutory right to collect campaign costs from the candidates, because to allow the right of collection would discriminate against less affluent candidates.

 

1917 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Baldassari v. United States


Civ. No. 16871


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


79 Cal. App. 3d 267; 144 Cal. Rptr. 741; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1512; 78-2 U.S. Tax Cas. (CCH) P9560; 42 A.F.T.R.2d (RIA) 5288



March 30, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Placer County, No. 46216, Ronald G. Cameron, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court for trial on the issues framed by the complaint, the answer and any pretrial order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant federal government sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Placer County (California), which granted summary judgment to respondent wife and son, decreeing that federal nominee tax liens on respondents' land were invalid as a matter of law, in an action to quiet title to respondents' land.


OVERVIEW: Respondents were the wife and son of a tax debtor, and respondents owned two parcels of land once held by debtor. To secure debtor's tax debt, the IRS filed liens on respondents' parcels of land as debtor's nominees. Respondents filed an action to quiet title to the land, and challenged the IRS liens. The trial court granted respondents' motion for summary judgment and held that even though the IRS had the right to file nominee liens, that respondents were denied due process because there was no hearing prior to the attachment of the lien. Appellant federal government sought review and the court reversed and held that the statutory pattern of the internal revenue laws authorized the filing of nominee liens when the IRS had reasonable cause to believe that the property against which the lien was filed might have been transferred to a third party to avoid creditors, including the federal government, and that the trial court erred in finding that there was an unconstitutional seizure of respondents' property, and that summary judgment was granted improperly because respondents showed no statutory requirement of a hearing prior to filing the nominee lien.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and held that the internal revenue laws authorized the filing of nominee liens when the IRS reasonably believed the property had been transferred to avoid creditors, therefore it was erroneous for the trial court to find respondents' property was unconstitutionally seized, and that summary judgment was granted erroneously because respondents showed no statutory requirement of a hearing prior to filing the nominee lien.

 

1918 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Aguilar


Crim. No. 17502.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


80 Cal. App. 3d 58; 145 Cal. Rptr. 197; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1399



March 28, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 25, 1978, and matter re-trans. to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 1, for reconsideration in the light of Young v. Superior Court, 21 C.A.3d 102, 109, and with directions to also reconsider whether the petition is moot. Opinion filed June 16, 1978, not for publication.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner brought a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the validity of his conviction for willful failure to provide for a minor child, Cal. Penal Code § 270.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pleaded guilty to willful failure to provide for a minor child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 270. Petitioner brought a petition for a writ of habeas corpus challenging the conviction, arguing that § 270 was constitutionally infirm in its unequal and discriminatory classification based upon the sex of the offender. The court ruled that a conviction under an unconstitutional statute could be challenged collaterally, notwithstanding that the conviction was based upon a plea of guilty. Further, the court held that the provisions of § 270 imposing primary criminal liability upon the father for willful failure to provide for a minor child, while holding the mother only secondarily liable, violated the equal protection guarantee of the state and federal constitutions. Also, the court found that the state's interest in securing support for minor children, and in relieving the public burden where parents were able to provide such support, was better served by a law which made each parent equally responsible. The court granted the petition for a writ of habeas corpus, concluding that proceedings based upon an unconstitutional statute were void and ineffectual.


OUTCOME: Petition for a writ of habeas corpus was granted, and the judgment convicting petitioner was vacated, where the challenged statute was constitutionally defective by reason of invidious discrimination against fathers of minor children.

1920 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Colbert)


Civ. No. 18128


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


78 Cal. App. 3d 1023; 144 Cal. Rptr. 599; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1366



March 21, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of mandate issue directing the superior court to set aside its order of March 13, 1978, insofar as it sustains the demurrer and strikes the special circumstance allegation from the amended information.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people petitioned for a writ of mandate to issue to respondent trial court, the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), where the real party in interest had been arraigned on an information charging murder, forcible rape, forcible sodomy, forcible oral copulation, and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury. The trial court granted the demurrer of the real party in interest and struck a special circumstance.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, the real party in interest, was arraigned on an information charging murder, forcible rape, forcible sodomy, forcible oral copulation, and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury. The information alleged a special circumstance relating to the charge of murder. Defendant demurred to special circumstance allegation and asserted that Cal. Const. art. I, § 27 was invalid because it effected a revision of the constitution. The trial court granted the demurrer and struck the special circumstance allegation. The court issued a writ of mandate, which required the trial court to set aside its order sustaining the demurrer and striking the special circumstance allegation. The trial court exceeded its jurisdiction in striking special circumstance allegation solely because of a misinterpretation of statutes. Confinement in prison for life without possibility of parole may not be imposed unless a special circumstance had been charged and found to be true. The invalidity of Cal. Const. art. I, § 27, and concomitant unconstitutionality of capital punishment provided no legal basis for precluding the imposition of a life sentence without possibility of parole.


OUTCOME: The court granted the people's request for a writ of mandate directing the trial court to set aside its order, insofar as it sustained the demurrer and struck the special circumstance allegation from the information because the trial court had exceeded its jurisdiction. The trial court erred in striking a special circumstance solely because of a misinterpretation of statutes.

 

1921 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bay Area Women's Coalition v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 41810


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


78 Cal. App. 3d 961; 144 Cal. Rptr. 591; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1361



March 21, 1978 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 718099, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, San Francisco City and County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction in favor of respondents, association and members, restraining enforcement of the five-year durational residency requirement provided in appellant's charter for persons appointed to appellant's boards and commissions.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, association and members, were women who participated in public affairs and had sought consideration for appointment to appellant city's board or commission. Respondents were precluded from appointments by a provision in appellant's charter that imposed a five-year durational residency requirement. Respondents filed an action seeking to enjoin appellant from enforcement of the provision and a declaration that the provision was unconstitutional. The trial court found in respondents' favor, granting the injunction. The court affirmed, holding that the residency requirement violated the equal protection clause under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court found that new residents were not a suspect class, but that strict scrutiny was applicable because the provision denied new residents their fundamental right to travel. The penalty imposed on the right to travel was the deprivation of the right to hold public office. Appellant could not show that it had a compelling government interest because ensuring that candidates had an understanding of local issues and a vested interest in appellant could be accomplished by less drastic means.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court because the residency requirement violated the equal protection clause. The court applied the strict scrutiny standard because the requirement deprived new residents of their fundamental right to travel. Appellant city could not show a compelling government interest because its objectives could be accomplished by less drastic measures.

 

1922 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kievlan v. Dahlberg Electronics, Inc.


Civ. No. 40017


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


78 Cal. App. 3d 951; 144 Cal. Rptr. 585; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1360



March 21, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 14, 1978, and the Petition of the Defendants and Appellants for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 15, 1978. Bird, C. J., did not Participate therein. Manuel, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 51868, Harry F. Brauer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed insofar as it does not provide for attorney's fees and the cause is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings on this issue only. In all other respects the judgment is affirmed. Plaintiffs to recover costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, manufacturer and distributor of hearing aids, sought review of the order of the Superior Court, Santa Cruz County (California), which permanently enjoined them from advertising their product by representing that it had a curative or therapeutic effect on diseases or disorders of the ear, and plaintiff individuals sought review of that part of the judgment which denied them attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: In a false advertising action brought by plaintiff individuals, the trial court permanently enjoined defendants, manufacturer and distributor of hearing aids, from advertising that their products had a curative or therapeutic effect. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that a hearing aid was a device within Cal. Health & Safety Code § 26009, there was no exemption for a compensatory effect in Cal. Health & Safety Code § 26463, and the injunction was proper because the advertisements were misleading by claiming the hearing aids had a curative or therapeutic effect on hearing loss. The court held U.S.C.S. § 360k did not preempt the field because Cal. Health & Safety Code § 26463 did not meet the first two elements of preemption: § 26463 contained a flat prohibition, not a requirement, and there was no showing that hearing aids were classified as restricted devices. The court reversed the denial of attorney fees for plaintiffs and held Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5 was applicable although it was not in effect at the time of trial because a lawsuit was governed by changes in procedural rules made during its pendency, which lasted until its final determination on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order permanently enjoining defendants, manufacturer and distributor of hearing aids, from advertising that their product had a curative or therapeutic effect on ear diseases or hearing disorders and reversed the order denying plaintiffs individuals attorney fees pursuant to a statute enacted during the pendency of the litigation.

People v. McIntire


Crim. No. 30883


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


78 Cal. App. 3d 844; 144 Cal. Rptr. 373; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1351



March 17, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 11, 1978 (see 23 C.3d 742)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a trial court (California) of a violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11360(a), sale of marijuana, after a jury verdict of guilty. Defendant appealed from an order of the trial court granting probation. The most important question on appeal was whether the trial court should have allowed evidence to be introduced relevant to entrapment and should have given requested defense instructions on entrapment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted that a doctrine of second-degree entrapment applied to her case. Defendant stated that her brother was entrapped by the police and that the police officer then persuaded her brother to place the idea of committing the crime in her mind where there was no preexisting intent. Defendant, however, was convicted of the sale of marijuana after a jury verdict of guilty and received probation. Defendant appealed from an order of the California trial court granting probation. The primary question on appeal was whether the trial court should have allowed evidence to be introduced relevant to entrapment and should have given requested defense instructions on entrapment. The court found the entrapment advanced by the defendant to be a type of second-degree entrapment that was advanced in dictum of two California cases, subsequently rejected by all courts where such a defense was attempted. Concluding that entrapment existed as a defense to prevent the police or their agents from using persuasive wiles to lead an otherwise innocent person astray, the court affirmed the trial court order sentencing defendant to probation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order granting probation to defendant for conviction of the sale of marijuana. The court rejected defendant's assertion of a doctrine of second-degree entrapment. The court noted that the dictum relied upon by defendant in former cases was repudiated by other courts reviewing cases brought under a similar theory of entrapment, and that entrapment existed as a defense as a check on police authority.

 

1924 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Morro Hills Community Services Dist. v. Board of Supervisors


Civ. No. 14713


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


78 Cal. App. 3d 765; 144 Cal. Rptr. 778; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1347



March 16, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 25, 1978. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 3068-N, Charles W. Froehlich, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Board of Supervisors of San Diego County appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) which held that defendant's resolution approving a proposed detachment of property from plaintiff Morro Hills Community Services District was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant County of San Diego Board of Supervisors adopted a resolution for detachment of certain land from plaintiff Morro Hills Community Services District subject to a condition which would relieve that property from the obligation of existing bonded indebtedness unless it reallocated the obligation to an affected city, county or district. Plaintiff filed a suit for declaratory relief, challenging the validity of defendant's action under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 860 et seq., and alleging that defendant abused its discretion in adopting the resolution. The trial court held that the detachment, conditioned as it was here, was invalid. On defendant's appeal, the court reversed. Interpreting Cal. Gov't Code §§ 56470(c) and 56492, the court found that the condition exempted the property from taxes, including taxes for payment of the bonded indebtedness, but did not eliminate the property's being subjected to liability, where otherwise necessary for payment of the bond obligation. The court further found substantial evidence to support defendant's action.


OUTCOME: Finding that defendant board's resolution for detachment of land from plaintiff community services district was valid, the court reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of plaintiff.

 

1925 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Brown


Crim. No. 17048


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


78 Cal. App. 3d 647; 143 Cal. Rptr. 549; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1336



March 15, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded to the trial court with directions to order the Community Release Board to promptly determine petitioner's length of time of imprisonment pursuant to the provisions of the Uniform Determinate Sentencing Act of 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Marin County (California) that invalidated the three-year minimum eligibility period for parole of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11360(a) as unconstitutional after petitioner prisoner sought a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner prisoner sought a writ of habeas corpus, contending that the penalty provision of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11360(a) under which he was sentenced was unconstitutional insofar as it provided for a three-year minimum period for eligibility for parole. Petitioner was sentenced to prison on September 26, 1975, and all of the trial court's proceedings were completed before July 1, 1977, the operative date of the Uniform Determinate Sentencing Act of 1976 (the Act), Cal. Penal Code § 1170 et seq. The trial court agreed with petitioner and declared that provision of § 11360 unconstitutional. The people appealed. The court held that the trial court's judgment was either moot or premature. The court held that under Cal. Penal Code § 1170.2(a) of the Act, petitioner was entitled to a review by the California Community Release Board (board) to determine what the length of time of imprisonment would have been under § 1170. The court held that the Act applied retroactively. The court reversed the judgment and remanded the matter to the trial court for a determination by the board of petitioner's length of time in prison and eligibility for parole under the provisions of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that found mandatory prison time before parole to be unconstitutional. The court held that the issue was either moot or premature because petitioner prisoner was entitled to have his sentence reviewed by the California Community Release Board under the retroactive provisions of the new sentencing statute.

 

1926 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Lee


Crim. No. 9452


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


78 Cal. App. 3d 753; 144 Cal. Rptr. 528; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1346



March 15, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 11, 1978.



DISPOSITION: We conclude that petitioners' commitment to the San Joaquin County jail pending hearing of their release applications was invalid. The orders to show cause have served their purpose. They are discharged and the writs of habeas corpus are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, inmates found not guilty by reason of insanity, sought habeas corpus review of their commitments to county jail pending hearing of their applications for their release from civil commitment, under Cal. Penal Code § 1026.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, inmates found not guilty by reason of insanity, were involuntarily committed. Petitioners applied for release but were moved to a county jail pending the restoration-to-sanity hearing. Petitioners sought a habeas corpus review of their confinement in county jail. The court denied the writs because petitioners had previously been released from custody. The court ruled, however, that jail confinement violated petitioners' right to protection against unjust confinement. While the court observed that Cal. Penal Code § 1026 appeared to provide for jail confinement during the hearing and evaluation process, the court ruled that the statute's reliance on other procedures set forth by the Lanterman-Petris-Short Act (LPS), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5000 et seq., for dealing with those involuntarily committed, indicated legislative intent to follow LPS procedures. Under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5150, the court observed that petitioners, while undergoing the evaluation and treatment process, could only have been confined in a treatment and evaluation facility approved by the state. Consequently, the court ruled that only confinement in such a facility would have been proper.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petitions for a writ of habeas corpus because petitioners had already been released from jail custody. The court ruled, however, that the county's practice of confining an individual awaiting a return-to-sanity hearing in the county jail was unconstitutional.

 

1927 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 16908


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


78 Cal. App. 3d 592; 144 Cal. Rptr. 504; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1330



March 14, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 11, 1978. Tobriner, J., and Newman, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 28729, William H. Woodward, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant offenders sought review, by writ of mandate, of a judgment of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which charged them with violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11550 for use and being under the influence of heroin, and punished appellants with the mandatory 90 days in county jail.


OVERVIEW: Appellant offenders challenged the mandatory 90-day county jail punishment as a condition of probation for their violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11550 for use and being under the influence of heroin. Appellants argued that the mandatory punishment was cruel and unusual punishment and violated their right to equal protection. The court affirmed the judgment and held that the mandatory sentence did not shock the conscience or offend fundamental notions of human dignity. The court held that the punishment for violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11550 applied to first offenders and contained no enhancement for recidivists, and it was of such short duration that it could not be deemed substantially excessive to fulfill the legitimate penological objectives of deterrence and isolation when the seriousness of the problem of drug use was considered. The court held that there was no equal protection violation because persons convicted of different crimes had no right to be treated equally.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because appellant offenders were not denied equal protection of the law and a 90-day mandatory sentence for use or being under the influence of a controlled substance was not cruel and unusual punishment.

 

1928 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hays v. Wood


Civ. No. 40623


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


78 Cal. App. 3d 354; 144 Cal. Rptr. 456; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1313



Mar. 8, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] as modified April 7, 1978, on denial of petition for rehearing.
Hg. granted May 4, 1978 (see 25 C.3d 772)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged an order of the trial court, California, requiring appellant to disclose the name of persons who paid up to a certain amount to his private entity, pursuant to the disclosure requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 87207.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city official and other officers commenced this action to compel appellant attorney to file a disclosure of income statement, as required by Cal. Gov't Code § 87207. Appellant, who was also a city councilman, alleged that the information sought was privileged. By way of a cross-complaint for declaratory relief, appellant sought to establish the unconstitutionality of the statute. The trial court declared the disclosure provisions valid and ordered appellant to comply. On appeal, the court determined that the provisions requiring appellant, as an attorney, to list the names of each person who paid fees of certain amount to his private entity was unconstitutional. The provisions amounted to an impermissible distinction between public officials who were attorneys or brokers and those engaged in other business activities or profession. The court reversed the judgment. To preserve the disclosure ends of the statute, it subjected appellant as an attorney to the general disclosure of other professionals.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order requiring appellant attorney to comply with the disclosure requirements of the statute, because it was unconstitutional. The provisions created an impermissible distinction between public officials who were attorneys or brokers and those who engaged in other professions.

 

1929 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gliksman


Crim. No. 9303


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


78 Cal. App. 3d 343; 144 Cal. Rptr. 451; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1312



March 7, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 23, 1978, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 1, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. C 36470, H. Walter Steiner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted him of two counts of receiving or offering to receive a bribe by a witness, Cal. Penal Code §138.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of receiving or offering to receive a bribe by a witness under Cal. Penal Code §138. Defendant sought review, contending that the jury instruction given by the court to define an agreement to receive a bribe was improper and Cal. Penal Code § 138 required the People to prove the existence of a mutual understanding. The reviewing court affirmed the holding of the lower court. The court rejected defendant's contentions, finding that the language of Cal. Penal Code § 138, "upon any understanding that his testimony shall be influenced thereby," meant that was the condition upon which the bribe was received or the offer to receive was made. When the bribe was received or the offer was made on that condition, express or implied, the crime was committed. The court noted that the state of mind of the actual or potential bribe-receiver was determinative; a bilateral agreement was not necessary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court that convicted defendant of two counts of receiving or offering to receive a bribe by a witness pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §138. The court held that the state of mind of the actual or potential bribe-receiver was determinative; a bilateral agreement was not necessary.

 

1930 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Medrano


Crim. No. 8962


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


78 Cal. App. 3d 198; 144 Cal. Rptr. 217; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1297; 98 L.R.R.M. 2829; 86 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,207



March 2, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied March 20, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Municipal Court for the Manteca-Ripon-Escalon-Tracy Judicial District of San Joaquin County, No. 54-107-C, Frank A. Grande, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgments of conviction are reversed with the instruction to dismiss the complaint.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant union organizers challenged their conviction by the Municipal Court for the Manteca-Ripon-Escalon-Tracy Judicial District of San Joaquin County (California), for criminal trespass. Defendants argued that Cal. Lab. Code § 1140 et seq., divested the municipal court of jurisdiction, and that their inability to enter a farm labor camp violated the free speech rights that inured to them and to the farm workers they sought to address.


OVERVIEW: Defendant union organizers entered a farm labor camp operated by a farm labor contractor to ask if residents needed transportation to election polling sites. Defendants were found guilty trespass. On appeal, defendants argued that the Agricultural Labor Relations Act of 1975 (ALRA), Cal. Lab. Code § 1140 et seq., divested the municipal court of jurisdiction, and that their constitutional rights and those of the farm workers they sought to address were infringed. The court held that jurisdiction was proper, but that the trespass law could not be constitutionally applied. No unfair labor practice occurred, because the ALRA expressly excluded farm labor contractors, and the camp operator was not the special emissary of a farm operator. The court also concluded that the camp operator's property interests were outweighed by constitutional free speech concerns, since no alternative effective means of communicating with the camp residents existed, and defendants' entry into the camp was brief. The absence of an essential element of trespass with respect to the defendant who was not asked to leave created independent grounds for reversal. Accordingly, the judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: Defendant union organizers' conviction for criminal trespass was reversed. Jurisdiction was proper, but the constitutional right to free speech was violated when defendants were denied access to a farm labor camp, because no effective alternative way was available for them to communicate with resident farm workers about an upcoming union election.

 

1931 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re S.


Crim. No. 31198


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


78 Cal. App. 3d 105; 144 Cal. Rptr. 204; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1287



February 28, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As modified Mar. 7 and Mar. 20, 1978
Hg. den. May 18, 1978, and the Reporter of Decisions is directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Appellate Reports (Cal. Const., art VI, § 14, Rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.)



DISPOSITION: The opinion of the court having been so modified, the petitions of appellant and of respondent for rehearings are denied March 20, 1978.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the juvenile court (California) that sustained charges for driving a vehicle without the owner's consent and receiving a credit card that was stolen property. Defendant claimed that the stolen property charge should have been brought under a code provision that specifically involved credit cards, and that the disposition order incorrectly allowed confinement for a period that exceeded his 21st birthday.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was adjudicated a ward of the state and recommitted to the Youth Authority after the juvenile court sustained charges for driving a vehicle without the owner's consent and receiving a credit card that was stolen property. On appeal, defendant argued that he should have been charged under a more specific section of the penal code that involved credit cards, and that the length of the confinement allowed in the disposition order was improper. The court held that prosecution under the more general statute for receiving stolen property was appropriate. The court reasoned that the legislature did not distinguish between general or special statutes, as long as the criminal activities charged were within the proscribed scope of such enactments. The court also concluded that the judgment required modification because the maximum period of defendant's confinement, calculated according to applicable determinate sentencing laws, exceeded the jurisdictional limit of his 21st birthday. Accordingly, the disposition order was modified to recite that defendant not be held beyond his 21st birthday, and the judgment as modified was affirmed.


OUTCOME: Judgment of conviction was affirmed, because no distinction existed between general or special statutes, as long as the criminal acts for which a defendant was charged fell within the proscribed scope of a legislative enactment. The sentence was modified, because the maximum period of confinement permitted by the consecutive sentences imposed upon defendant went past his 21st birthday, and exceeded the juvenile court's jurisdictional limit.

 

1932 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Gonzales)


Civ. No. 52412


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


78 Cal. App. 3d 134; 144 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1290



February 28, 1978 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent to vacate its order of September 20, 1977, sentencing real party to state prison for a term of six months to two years in that matter entitled People of the State of California v. Philip Gonzales Gonzales, Los Angeles Superior Court No. A516305, and to conduct a new sentencing hearing. If respondent thereafter concludes that a state prison sentence is appropriate, it shall sentence real party to state prison for the term prescribed by law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the state, sought a writ of mandate after respondent, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, and not the community release board, sentenced the real party in interest to a specific term of imprisonment contrary to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1168(b), 1170(a)(2), and 1170.2(a) and (b) (1977).


OVERVIEW: After respondent, the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, sentenced the real party in interest to imprisonment for six months to two years following a probation violation for a crime committed in 1973, petitioner, the state, sought a writ of mandate. Petitioner contended that respondent's judgment erroneously specified the length of the prison term rather than sentencing "the term prescribed by law" according to Cal. Penal Code § 1168(b) and § 1170(a)(2) (1977) and that, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1170.2(a) and (b) (1977), the community release board (CRB), not respondent, was responsible for fixing the imprisonment term. The court issued an alternative writ, vacated the order, and instructed that a new sentencing hearing be held. It noted that the legislature amended §§ 1170 and 1170.2 in order to make the date of the criminal act determinative. Thus, defendants sentenced after July 1, 1977 for crimes committed prior to that date should have imprisonment terms fixed by the CRB. This approach did not improperly delegate powers because the legislative body provided the sentence, the judicial branch imposed it, and the administrative body simply carried it out.


OUTCOME: The court granted the request of petitioner, the state, to issue a peremptory writ of mandate. The court directed respondent superior court to vacate its order sentencing real party in interest to prison for six months to two years and to conduct a new sentencing hearing. It was constitutionally sound for statutory law to empower the community release board, not respondent, to fix imprisonment terms.

 

1933 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 51201


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


77 Cal. App. 3d 961; 143 Cal. Rptr. 860; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1273



February 24, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] as modified Mar. 23, 1978, on denial of petition for rehearing.
Hg. granted Apr. 20, 1978 (see 22 C.3d 731)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged the judgment of the superior court (California), which dismissed appellant's cause of action against respondent county for breach of employment contract. Appellant argued his dismissal was without just cause and that he was denied due process.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was a seasonal employee of respondent county. Appellant was fired, and filed suit against respondent for dismissal without just cause and for a declaratory judgment that the failure to accord seasonal employees the same hearing upon dismissal as permanent employees was unconstitutional. The trial court granted respondent's demurrer and dismissed the case. Appellant challenged the dismissal. The court affirmed, and held that the discharge was proper pursuant to respondent's rules regarding seasonal employees, which did not require just cause for discharge. The court held that a seasonal employee's interest in his job was not the kind of interest which invoked the constitutional requirements of hearing and due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal, and held that respondent county did not have to have just cause to dismiss a seasonal employee and that a seasonal employee's interest in his job was not the kind of interest which invoked due process considerations.

 

1934 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wyrick


Crim. No. 9118


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


77 Cal. App. 3d 903; 144 Cal. Rptr. 38; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1267



February 23, 1978 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 17, 1978, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 19, 1978.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tehama County, No. 3119, Richard B. Eaton, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the motion setting aside the indictment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed the order of the Superior Court of Tehama County (California) which granted defendant's motion to quash his indictment for unlawfully recording a confidential communication without the permission of both parties to the conversation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 632. The trial court dismissed the indictment and ruled that § 632 was unconstitutionally vague. The trial court also ruled that the recorded conversation was not considered or intended to be confidential within the meaning of the statute. The court reversed the dismissal of the indictment and held that when measured by the standard of a person of ordinary intelligence, the statute did not suffer from unconstitutional vagueness. The court held that the subjective expectations of the parties, as those expectations bore upon probable re-communication of the conversation, were irrelevant. The confidential communication that was recorded without consent constituted a violation of § 632.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the indictment of defendant and held that the state statute was not unconstitutionally vague, and that the recording of a confidential communication without the consent of all the parties was a violation of the state statute.

Tex-Cal Land Management, Inc. v. Agricultural Labor Relations Board


Civ. No. 3395


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


77 Cal. App. 3d 794; 144 Cal. Rptr. 149; 1978 Cal. App. LEXIS 1257; 84 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P55,142



Feb. 21, 1978 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted May 10, 1978 (See 24 C.3d 335)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review from respondent, Agricultural Labor Relations Board (California), which ordered petitioner to cease and desist from engaging in unfair labor practices in cause of action brought by real party in interest, United Farm Workers of America, on behalf of employees.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, United Farm Workers of America, filed several unfair labor practices charges against petitioner employer. An administrative law officer recommended to respondent, Agricultural Labor Relations Board, that petitioner had committed various unfair labor practices, and respondent ordered petitioner to cease and desist from engaging in the unfair labor practices. Petitioner sought review. The court examined the constitutionality of Cal. Lab. Code §§ 1160.3 and 1160.8, which provided for adjudicative powers vested in respondent and judicial review of respondent's orders by an appellate court rather than a superior court. The court held that respondent was empowered to act as a judicial tribunal to adjudicate agricultural labor disputes, pursuant to the Agricultural Labor Relations Act, Cal. Lab. Code § 1140, et seq. The court determined that it was permissible for the legislature to vest judicial review in an appellate court instead of a trial court. The court held that petitioner had committed unfair labor practices and that the reading of notices for illiterate employees should be required. The court affirmed as modified the order of respondent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed as modified the order of respondent, Agricultural Labor Relations Board, which ordered petitioner employer to cease and desist from engaging in unfair labor practices. The court held that, pursuant to the Agricultural Labor Relations Act, respondent was empowered to act as a judicial tribunal to adjudicate disputes and that it was permissible to vest judicial review in an appellate court instead of a trial court.
