On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that the permanent injunction was properly dissolved and the statutory prohibition on optical price advertising was unconstitutional. The court noted that appellant should be exonerated from the contempt order. The court held that appellant had no action against respondent association for damages resulting from the wrongful issuance of the injunction, and that respondent boards were protected by governmental immunity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's judgment against appellant optical firm, holding that the permanent injunction was properly dissolved and the statutory prohibition on optical price advertising was unconstitutional. The court noted that appellant should be exonerated from the contempt order, but had no action to recover damages for the wrongful issuance of the injunction.

 

1990 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Lucido v. Rippeto


Civ. No. 39612


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


73 Cal. App. 3d 1; 140 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1791



August 31, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 20, 1977, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 3, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 198853, Thomas M. Jenkins, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff welfare recipient appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which denied his petition for a peremptory writ of mandate ordering the payment of his claim for benefits without deductions for recoupment for overpayments based solely on county error, and denied his prayer for a declaration that the regulation authorizing the deduction was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff welfare recipient received welfare assistance from defendant county during a period when the value of his life insurance policy made him ineligible. Defendant discovered its error and instituted recoupment of those funds when plaintiff later reapplied and was granted benefits. Plaintiff filed a writ of mandate for recovery of his reduction in benefits and for a declaration that San Mateo County, Cal. General Assistance Manual § 4-07, which authorized that deduction was invalid. He argued that the common law rule against recoupment of welfare benefits applied when no statute authorized it; that the regulation defendant relied on was invalid because it contravened Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 17000; and that defendant was estopped from seeking recoupment because its own error caused the overpayment. The trial court rejected plaintiff's contentions. Plaintiff sought review and the court affirmed the trial court's judgment stating that a regulation authorized recoupment and that because the reduction of benefits did not deny all necessary assistance, it was proper. Likewise, plaintiff's claim of estoppel was rejected because it required elements that were not shown to exist.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, stating that the rule defendant applied was properly adopted because the reduction of benefits did not deny all necessary assistance. The common law rule against recoupment of benefits was inapplicable citing the rule plaintiff relied on, and a failure to prove all of the elements of equitable estoppel barred its application.

 

1991 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Anderson


Crim. No. 29787


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


73 Cal. App. 3d 38; 140 Cal. Rptr. 546; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1796



August 31, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. M 240838, Marvin A. Freeman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people sought review of an order entered in a habeas corpus proceeding by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which required the department of health to hold a limited hearing before revoking respondent mental patient's status as an outpatient from a state hospital.


OVERVIEW: Respondent mental patient had been admitted to a state hospital under the provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1026 after his acquittal on an insanity plea. After he had been placed on outpatient status, he was returned to the hospital on the initiative of local mental health personnel. The people appealed from an order entered in a habeas corpus proceeding by the trial court, which required the department of health to hold a limited hearing before officially revoking respondent's status as an outpatient. The court affirmed the trial court. It reasoned that the conditional liberty interest of the mental outpatient, like that of a parolee and that of an outpatient from the California Rehabilitation Center, included many of the core values of unqualified liberty and could not be terminated without due process of law. It held that minimal notice and hearing requirements applied in the case. It also held that insofar as the language of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 7375 excluded the required notice and hearing, it failed to pass constitutional scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which required the department of health to hold a limited hearing before revoking respondent mental patient's status as an outpatient from a state hospital. It did so because the conditional liberty interest of the mental outpatient included many of the core values of unqualified liberty and could not be terminated without due process of law.

 

1992 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Superior Strut & Hanger Co. v. Port of Oakland


Civ. No. 39294


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


72 Cal. App. 3d 987; 140 Cal. Rptr. 515; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1787



August 29, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 28, 1977. Racanelli, J., * was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 27, 1977.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 445975-6, Robert K. Barber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The superior court is directed to modify the judgment by striking therefrom the award of attorneys' fees to respondent. As so modified, the judgment is affirmed. Each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Port of Oakland sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which awarded relocation costs and attorneys' fees to respondent tenant as a displaced person under the California Relocation Assistance Law, Cal. Gov't. Code § 7260 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Port of Oakland acquired property for public use and continued to lease the property to respondent tenant until November 7, 1972. Appellant successfully sought relocation costs and attorney's fees upon eviction under the California Relocation Assistance Law (Act), Cal. Gov't. Code § 7260 et seq. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment awarding relocation costs to respondent, but struck the award of attorneys' fees because they were not authorized by the Act. The court held that exercise of the power of eminent domain was not a prerequisite to relocation benefits because the Act only required that the property be acquired for public use. Respondent was eligible for relocation costs because his move was the direct result of appellant's purchase of the property for public use. Respondent was eligible for benefits because its moving date was after the Act's effective date. Despite the Act's language that gave appellant sole authority to decide benefits, the court found respondent had a right to judicial review of the statute and its constitutionality as interpreted by appellant. The court held that an 18-month limitation period applied to relocation claims.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and denied in part, directing the trial court to strike the award of attorneys' fees to respondent tenant because the fees were not authorized by the California Relocation Assistance Law, but affirmed the award of relocation costs as mandatory because appellant Port of Oakland acquired the property and asked respondent to vacate the property for public use after the effective date of the statute.

 

1993 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Los Angeles v. City of Artesia


Civ. No. 50389


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


73 Cal. App. 3d 450; 140 Cal. Rptr. 684; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1861



August 25, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 27, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C76041, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment for defendants is affirmed. Appellants to bear costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment on behalf of respondent county in a case regarding amounts to be paid under contract for police protection.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the lower court judgment that had granted summary judgment on behalf of respondent county because it found that appellant city failed show that there were triable issues of fact. The court held that summary judgment was proper because respondent provided affidavits in support of the motion and appellant's counter-affidavits did not create a triable issue of fact regarding the reasonableness of the cost reductions for the police protection service. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 51350 was not unconstitutional because it bore a rational relationship to a legitimate state purpose. The court held that the statute allowed for respondent to recover the amount represented by reductions in charges to the contracting cities, but that the cities were permitted to disavow the contracts each year and that this was not sufficient to show that the statute was unconstitutional. The court found that § 51350 did not have a retroactive application because the cost reductions did not apply retroactively to services already rendered but rather applied to services rendered after the effective date of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of respondent county because it found that appellant city had not shown that there was a triable issue of fact regarding the reasonableness of the cost reductions provided for under statute.

 

1994 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Peterson v. Long Beach


Civ. No. 49636.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


72 Cal. App. 3d 852; 140 Cal. Rptr. 401; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1774



August 24, 1977 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Nov. 3, 1977 (See 24 C.3d 238)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents challenged a judgment of the trial court (California), which denied recovery against respondent city and its police officer for the wrongful death of their son. In a bench trial, the court found that the officer acted reasonably and with good cause. Appellants alleged that the officer acted negligently and unreasonably in firing to prevent decedent's escape from the scene of what officers erroneously believed was a burglary.


OVERVIEW: Appellant parents challenged a judgment of the trial court, which denied recovery against respondent city and its police officer for the wrongful death of their son. Appellant's child was shot and killed as he fled the scene after refusing to obey a command to halt by officers who sought to apprehend him as a burglary suspect. Appellants alleged that the officer acted negligently and unreasonably in firing to prevent decedent's escape. In a bench trial, the court found that the officer acted reasonably and with good cause. On appeal, the court reversed on the basis of a recent decision squarely on point, which was issued by another intermediate appellate court and which was denied a hearing by the state supreme court. That decision held that a police officer was held to the same standard as a private citizen in using deadly force to arrest a felony suspect. Thus, deadly force may not be used against a fleeing suspect absent circumstances that reasonably create a fear of death or serious bodily harm to the officer or to another. Applying this holding to the facts of this case, the court agreed with appellants that the evidence established liability as a matter of law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, relying on an appellate court opinion filed while this appeal was pending, holding that the facts as found by the trial court did not justify the use of deadly force to apprehend the suspect in the absence of evidence that the purported "burglary in progress" was committed under circumstances creating a fear of great bodily harm. Therefore, the use of deadly force was excessive as a matter of law.

 

1995 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Aaron v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 39964


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


73 Cal. App. 3d 596; 140 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1874



August 24, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 347897, John S. McInerny, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court is reversed and the case is remanded with directions to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition commanding the respondent municipal court to refrain from further proceedings in the actions specified in the petition, pending against petitioners, other than to dismiss the same.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that denied their petition for a writ of prohibition that sought to prohibit their prosecution by respondent municipal court for the violation of a municipal ordinance which outlawed soliciting without a license.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were being prosecuted for violating a municipal ordinance that required that anyone soliciting anything of value for a charitable purpose to obtain a permit from the city. The ordinance provided that a permit could be issued only if the city's public solicitation commission was satisfied that the purpose of the solicitation was worthy and not incompatible with public interest, that the applicant was of good character, and that the total expenses of the solicitation were not disproportionate to the sum to be collected. The ordinance also allowed the commission to waive some of the requirements when the applicant was known to be a bona fide charitable organization. Petitioners asserted that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the petition for writ of prohibition filed by petitioners that sought to prohibit their prosecution. On appeal, the court reversed and directed the trial court to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition. The court held that the ordinance was too vague to constitute a constitutionally permissive control of charitable solicitation because it failed to set forth definite, objective guidelines for the issuance of permits.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that denied the petition for writ of prohibition. The ordinance at issue was unconstitutional because it failed to set forth definite, objective guidelines for the issuance of permits. The court remanded the case with directions to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition commanding respondent municipal court to refrain from further proceedings and to dismiss the action against petitioners.

 

1996 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


ITT Gilfillan, Inc. v. City of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 49209


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


72 Cal. App. 3d 421; 140 Cal. Rptr. 193; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1726; 24 Cont. Cas. Fed. (CCH) P81,679



August 4, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied September 1, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petitions of appellant Litton Systems, Inc., and respondent for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied October 13, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. C849721, C879550, Stevens Fargo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a judgment for defendant city, entered in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in their suits for a refund of taxes they paid pursuant to Los Angeles Business Tax Ordinance §§ 21.168.1, 21.190.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers manufactured and delivered military weapons, equipment, and supplies for the federal government. Some of their shipments were made on government bills of lading; others were made on commercial bills of lading. Plaintiffs reported the gross receipts from their government contracts to defendant city, but excluded the receipts attributable to property shipped outside the state by bills of lading. Defendant assessed tax deficiencies, which plaintiffs paid. Plaintiffs then brought complaints for refunds. The court reversed the trial court's order in favor of defendant. Plaintiffs were taxable on their gross receipts under Los Angeles Business Tax Ordinance §§ 21.166, 21.167, not under Los Angeles Business Tax Ordinance § 21.190, as defendant asserted; plaintiffs' processes of sale and service were commercially inseparable and could not be assessed separately. Plaintiffs were taxable on gross receipts for shipments on government bills of lading. These were not exempted by Los Angeles Business Tax Ordinance § 21.168.1, as were those shipments made on commercial bills of lading.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment for defendant city, holding that plaintiff taxpayers could be taxed on their gross receipts from government contracts but that they were not taxable under the higher rate of a catch-all ordinance provision. The court also held plaintiffs liable for gross receipts attributable to shipments to points outside the state on government bills of lading, but not on commercial bills of lading.

 

1997 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Simmons


Civ. No. 48616


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


72 Cal. App. 3d 205; 139 Cal. Rptr. 832; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1703



July 29, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 28, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D 856239, James N. Reese, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment under appeal is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review from a Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) judgment pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4702(a) denying her request that part of her monthly child support from respondent father be directed to an educational trust fund instead of going entirely to the county.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother sought to direct part of the monthly child support paid to appellant by respondent father to an educational trust instead of paying the entire amount to the county department of public social services. When the lower court entered an order pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 4702(a) denying that request, appellant sought review. The court found that aid to families with dependent children (AFDC) constituted welfare monies under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 10052, such that child support payments collected for AFDC purposes could not be paid directly to the child's mother. Appellant had assigned all of her accrued rights to the support of her minor child by respondent and therefore all of the child support payments had to be sent to the county department of public social services. Thus, the court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that appellant's child support constituted welfare monies and was not paid directly to her in any case; appellant had assigned all rights to that money away already, such that she had no further rights to assign.

 

1998 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garner


Crim. No. 29847


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


72 Cal. App. 3d 214; 139 Cal. Rptr. 838; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1705



July 29, 1977 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-327152, David N. Fitts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted defendant's motion to dismiss based on evidence that the police department and district attorney's office had a policy neither to arrest nor to prosecute bettors while they did arrest and prosecute bookmakers.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with bookmaking in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 337(a)(1), (2), (6). Defendant filed a motion to dismiss based on undisputed evidence that the police department and district attorney's office had a policy neither to arrest nor to prosecute bettors while they did arrest and prosecute bookmakers. The trial court found that because § 337(6) made it a crime to place as well as to receive a bet, the prosecution of the bookmaker alone and not the bettor as well constituted unconstitutional discriminatory enforcement of the statute in violation of U. S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 7(a). Plaintiff state challenged the judgment. The court reversed, holding that the selective enforcement of § 337 against bookmakers and not against bettors was not based on an invidious and unjustifiable standard. The court found that a distinction drawn on the basis of the difference in the kind of criminal conduct, even though of equal culpability under the law, was not suspect and that the selective enforcement of the statute against defendant was not a denial to her of constitutional equal protection.


OUTCOME: The order of dismissal was reversed because the selective enforcement of the statute against bookmakers and not against bettors did not deny defendant to her constitutional right of equal protection. A distinction drawn on the basis of the difference in the kind of criminal conduct, even though of equal culpability under the law, was not based on an invidious and unjustifiable standard.

 

1999 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Pasadena Unified Sch. Dist. v. Pasadena Federation of Teachers


Civ. No. 49576


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


72 Cal. App. 3d 100; 140 Cal. Rptr. 41; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1694; 96 L.R.R.M. 2363



July 28, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 18, 1977, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Hg. den. October 26, 1977. Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 25, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 124815, Thomas Wilfred LeSage, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff school district appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed plaintiff's action for damages against defendant union based on its alleged inducement of a public school employees' strike.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff school district filed an action against defendant union based on its inducement of a strike by plaintiff's employees. Defendant demurred, and the trial court dismissed the complaint for failing to state a cause of action. The court reversed because it was unlawful for public school employees to strike. Defendant's free speech rights would not be violated because restraint on speech was not unconstitutional where the union was inducing an illegal strike. The court determined that defendant was not privileged to induce the breach of contract because the object of the action was unlawful. The complaint stated a cause of action for tortious inducement of breach of contract and based on defendant's direct liability. Conducting an unlawful strike was an independent tort and there was liability for aiding and abetting an intentional tort. The Educational Employment Relations Board had no jurisdiction over the action, because it occurred prior to the effective date of the board's jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that dismissed plaintiff school district's action for damages against defendant union because plaintiff stated a cause of action against defendant union. The court held a public school employee strike was unlawful and defendant's inducement of such was neither privileged, nor protected as free speech.

 

2000 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


McGlothlen v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. Nos. 38438, 39690, 39691, 39692


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


71 Cal. App. 3d 1005; 140 Cal. Rptr. 168; 1977 Cal. App. LEXIS 1679



July 27, 1977 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied 71 Cal. App. 3d 1005 at 1028.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, Nos. 63558, 63548, 11211, 11210, Raymond J. Sherwin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded with directions to enter judgment for the Department of Motor Vehicles in each of the consolidated cases.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Motor Vehicles challenged the judgments of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which granted petitioner drivers' requests and ordered defendant and the municipal court to refrain from revoking petitioners' drivers licenses as a result of their second convictions for driving while under the influence of intoxicating liquor so long as they participated successfully in the county rehabilitation program.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner drivers were convicted of second offenses of driving while under the influence of intoxicating liquor (DWI), and they sought relief from the superior court to order defendant Department of Motor Vehicles and the municipal court to refrain from revoking their drivers licenses so long as they completed a rehabilitation program. The superior granted the relief, and thus, awarded them the benefits of Cal. Veh. Code §§ 13201.5 and 13352.5 despite the fact that the county was not approved for the demonstration project. Defendant sought review, claiming that the limitation of the project to four counties was not a denial of equal protection. The court found that the project did not involve a suspect class or a fundamental interest and that the differential treatment had a rational relationship to the project's purpose. Thus, it did not violate equal protection. Furthermore, the project was not arbitrary and was neither a general law which did to have a uniform operation nor an invalid local or special statute. Accordingly, the court held that the trial court erred in concluding petitioners were entitled to the project's benefit, and the judgments were reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments ordering defendant Department of Motor Vehicles to refrain from revoking the drivers licenses of petitioner drivers because the limitation of the demonstration project to four counties did not involve a suspect class or a fundamental interest and had a rational basis to the project's purpose. Thus, the project did not deny equal protection under either the federal or state constitutions.
